Significant Discoveries of Ancient Relics at the Yin Ruins 77

Earliest Sculpture … Polychrome Glazed Pottery

Bronze Molds and Vessels … Qilin Skull

Dr. Li Ji, Director of the National Institute of History and Philology in Beiping, a Harvard graduate and member of the Smithsonian Institution, has recently conducted scientific excavations in Anyang County, Henan, uncovering a rich array of Shang dynasty artifacts of considerable importance. The following article, penned by Mr. Edward Hunter, principal writer for the Peking English Guide based on Dr. Li’s remarks, is translated here for readers:

“Recent discoveries in Henan are of such value that several pages of China’s art, culture, and social history must be rewritten. The major finds are summarized below:

1. The discovery of China’s oldest sculpture, approximately 1,500 years earlier than previously estimated.

2. The unearthing of Shang dynasty polychrome glazed pottery (dating to 1766–1122 B.C.), with manufacture predating the Shang by at least 1,000 years, conclusively proving the earlier invention of glazed ceramics.

3. The first-ever discovery of Shang dynasty bronze molds and white bronze vessels, confirming that bronze mold technology was already in use during the Shang.

These findings demonstrate that 3,000 years ago the Chinese had a penchant for antiquities akin to modern connoisseurs. In addition, several hundred inscribed tortoise shells and ox scapulae used for divination were found, and inscriptions on bone fragments confirm that the mythical Qilin once existed as a single‑horned beast of formidable nature.

Excavation took place in a small township of Anyang County, formerly under Zhangde Prefecture, site of the ancient Shang capital. Despite unrest in the Central Plains, work continued uninterrupted until recently, when bandit raids and local villagers’ disregard for Feng Shui temporarily halted operations. The ruins were first discovered in 1899 (Guangxu year 25), yielding several inscribed bones. Since then excavations have been sporadic, but most artifacts suffered damage due to the unscientific efforts of local farmers and antique dealers seeking a few bone fragments for sale to foreign museums. These inscriptions, over 4,000 years old, are China’s earliest script. Only in the past few years have detailed excavations been undertaken, and most major finds have emerged in the last several months. The principal artifacts are now housed at the Beihai History Research Institute. Detailed important discoveries follow:

(1) Sculpture: Discovered by Dr. Li Ji, the piece—broken into three sections but largely intact when reassembled—depicts a crouching human figure. Only the torso to lower legs survive. The figure stands several feet tall, the hands clasping the legs, with floral patterns carved on the arms in an ancient style. Its patina is characteristic of Shang craftsmanship, dating at least 3,200 years ago. This predates any known Chinese sculpture by 1,500 years and is unlike Babylonian or Egyptian works predating the Shang.

(2) Ceramics: One vessel is coated with a vitreous glaze—an extraordinary find. It is clearly not Shang in style, suggesting it may have been brought from elsewhere. Dr. Li speculates it belonged to an antiquities collector of the period, dating roughly 1,000 years before the Shang. The discovery site lies in a floodplain that was once part of the Yellow River’s course; geologists identify four major floods there. Early glaze technology of this age is significant, as glaze invention is generally attributed to the Han dynasty (from ca. 200 B.C.).

(3) Bronze Molds and White Bronze Vessels: An astonishing discovery. While some scholars had speculated the Shang used white bronze, this provides the first solid evidence. The bronze molds, invented in the Shang, and Shang jade artifacts were also found. Although the Zhou dynasty (1122–255 B.C.) introduced a new social order, its artistic traditions largely continued Shang practices.

(4) Tortoise Plastrons and Ox Scapulae: These bones exhibit small drill holes and charcoal traces, indicating their use in oracle bone divination. Last autumn’s finds also included bronze bowls, arrowheads, and spearheads—all Shang artifacts—alongside stone carvings resembling Zhou jade, suggesting Zhou artisans inherited Shang designs. Many carved stone balls and a finely crafted jade axe were recovered, as well as additional white jade objects from the Shang.

(5) Qilin: An animal skull bearing inscriptions unexpectedly confirmed that the Qilin once existed as a one‑horned beast. Marco Polo’s descriptions of the Qilin align with these findings, lending credence to his accounts.”

Local Correspondence 78

Songjiang

The Songjiang Chinese Medical Association held its re‑election earlier in January, producing seven executive committee members: Zhu Wenqing, Jiang Anren, Zha Gongfu, Yang Yunquan, Zhou Chungku, Han Fengjiu, and Nie Yufang. On the fifteenth of this month, they met again to elect the standing committee from among themselves, resulting in Jiang Anren’s selection. At an extraordinary meeting yesterday, they formally assumed their duties.

In the Zhoujia Grass area just outside the county’s North Gate near Longxing Bridge, some people from north of the river have been dismantling tomb enclosures and excavating graves. The other day, they uncovered a tomb and found several stone slabs engraved with inscriptions. After local scholars washed and rubbed them clean, they realized it was the Song Dynasty tomb of Zhou Tong; these slabs form his tomb epitaph. Locals immediately halted the excavation, and the slabs are now being prepared for preservation. According to records, Zhou Tong was the teacher of General Yue Fei, and the book “Yue’s Biography” contains many accounts of his deeds.

The Civil Affairs Department has dispatched Commissioner Huang Chao (a native of Chuxiong, Zhejiang) to Songjiang. He arrived today, immediately called on County Magistrate Jin, and met with local commerce leaders, who indicated they are still conducting a covert inquiry into the case of Wu Qinian’s disruption of the marketplace.

Qingpu

Grandmother Despairs After Granddaughter’s Disappearance. Behind the Bieyoutian Teahouse on Matou Street resides Ms. Jiang Zhu, over sixty years old. She has one son whose wife, Ms. Wang, bore a single daughter, now seventeen, named A‑Xiu. Last autumn, a matchmaker arranged for A‑Xiu to become a son‑in‑law (through purchase) to Ji Bosheng of Nanxiang, who works as a partner in a local distillery. Upon entering the family, he took the name Jiang Baisun. The couple became fond of each other and pressed for an early wedding, but A‑Xiu’s family lacked funds and deferred the marriage for three years or until Ji secured 300 yuan abroad. Impatient and penniless, Ji assaulted his fiancée and fled to Nanxiang. Since A‑Xiu’s disappearance, Ms. Jiang has hired searchers to no avail, weeping day and night. At dusk the other day, she attempted suicide but was discovered by her son and rescued before losing her life.

Bandits Raid Zhou De’s Farmhouse. In Chenqiaojiang Village, Qingpu County, farming couple Zhou De and his wife were attacked around noon yesterday by a large group of bandits who forced open their door. When asked, “Where is your rifle?” they replied they had sold it; asked “Where is your son?” they feigned ignorance. The bandits bound the couple to the bed, covered their faces with bedclothes, and then spoke in a local accent, revealing themselves as villagers. After killing them, they drank at the scene until three o’clock, then escaped southward. They stole 154 silver dollars, over eighty bolts of small cloth, and numerous pieces of jewelry and clothing.

Fengxian

Rewards and Punishments in the Eastern Office Jailbreak Case. The county prison under the Eastern Office holds over two hundred inmates, including court‑held prisoners. On February 5, the chief suspects in the Communist bandits’ murder of former township head Zhang Peilin took advantage of guards shouting and drinking to tunnel through the rear wall and slither out. More than ten attempted flight north toward Nanhui but were spotted at the North Gate by police sentry Zang Xuezhi. He arrested them on the spot and delivered them safely to headquarters. Prefect Hong has officially commended Squad Leader Jin Zhenzhong and promoted Patrol Officer Zang to acting sergeant. Prison warden Lu, blamed for lax security and failure to investigate, has been dismissed pending High Court discipline. Duty guards have also been referred for prosecution, and the escapees, including Gu Futang, have been ordered by the High Court to be executed.

Exposure of a Communist Cell. In the Fourth District’s Jinjia Qigan area, Zhang Sandì—former cadre of the banned Communist branch “Dawn Light Middle School”—frequently met with teacher Liu Qinzai and held clandestine meetings at his home. County Police Captain Ding Zhiyan, with Squad Leader Tu Qinfang and others, arrested Zhang Sandì and his son, along with comrades Kang Musheng and Zhu Guijiang. All four confessed to Communist activity with Liu. The county has placed Liu Qinzai on the provincial wanted list, and the four suspects will be sent to the High Court for trial.

Jiading

Refugees Arrested Across the Border Injured. Fifteen members of Chen Ayou’s party from Luoyang Township—arrested by Shanghai Special Municipal Public Security Bureau, District 3, across the border—were returned to the county. On the afternoon of the eighteenth, Presiding Magistrate Zhang held a hearing. They all testified that Luoyang Village is far from the incident site; that at night they heard gongs, police broke in, dragged them from their beds, and beat them along the way and at the station. Qian Fangchu immediately vomited blood; Qian Nairong and others were injured and feverish. Medical examinations showed wounds of varying severity; the six most serious, including Qian Fangchu, were sent to Sun Yat‑sen Hospital, while the other nine remain detained awaiting further medical inspection.

Suzhou

Armed Robbery at Qiantai Cheng Lumber Store Outside Xumen Gate. At No. 47 Zaoshi Street, Qiantai Cheng’s wood shop was thriving. Around 8:30 pm on the nineteenth, just after dinner, two bandits burst in—one in a long satin robe and velvet cap carrying a flashlight, the other in a short jacket with a pistol. They barred the door, forced partners and apprentices into one room, then pried open the cash chest, stealing 435 yuan in bills, 161 yuan in counterfeit notes, 335 silver dollars, and 5.4 yuan in small silver. Partner Wang Xi’an hid and slipped out to alert the Seventh Precinct. Inspector Wang notified the Min Police Department and led a two‑pronged pursuit. The robbers fled west, dividing the loot en route, crossed Zaoshi Bridge, and turned east, encountering the Rapid Response Team. At Taixing Bridge, the robber in the long robe evaded capture, eluding officers even before the Seventh Precinct. Inspector Wang chased him to Dahrigh Bridge, where the robber discarded his pistol into the river and hid in a nearby thatch hut. Sergeant Liu Jun’s flashlight revealed one shoe, leading to the robber’s capture. On him were recovered 313 yuan in genuine notes, all 161 yuan in counterfeit, 13 silver dollars, a cigarette case, a gold watch chain (about six qian), a watch, and a three‑inch bronze Buddha—verified by Qiantai Cheng’s partners as stolen goods. Detective Captain Zhou Hongjiang and precinct sergeants then brought the robber to the district office. Commissioner Zhang’s interrogation yielded no confession until further questioning by Captain Zhou, when he admitted the robbery and implicated another accomplice surnamed Jia at the Tongrenchang factory. Officers escorted the robber back to the district office overnight and were dispatched to arrest the fugitive, but so far without success. The suspect was returned to the station at 9 am yesterday for processing. Meanwhile, Inspector Wang also arrested three suspects—Gao Jinju, Zhao Youbi, and Zhu Hongfu—at a thatch shelter south of the Xumen Gate Taixiang Bridge, seizing a coded slip, a passport, and promissory notes; they were sent to the district office for related questioning.

Another Batch of Refugees Arrives in Suzhou. Yesterday (20th) at 9 am, over one hundred refugees from Pizhou County, sent from the Wujiang area, arrived in Suzhou. The precinct reported to the commissioner, and Director Zou of the Municipal Public Security Bureau dispatched officers to facilitate their onward departure.

Military Servants Collude in Theft and Escape. Paymaster Qian Dajun of the Wuchang Military Officer School recently cashed his salary of 2,600 yuan. His servants Si Huagui and Huang Rong colluded to steal the money and abscond. Si Huagui was captured by Hankou Special Police agents. Huang Rong, residing at No. 18 Shipihe Lane in Suzhou, was named by Qian in a telegram to the Suzhou Public Security Bureau. Director Zou ordered the detective squad yesterday (20th) to arrest Huang’s brother, Huang Daxing, and bring him in for questioning.

Charitable Donations Used to Launch Initiatives. In Wu County, following charitable donations, the county was ordered to allocate funds to education. Yesterday (20th), the Education Bureau sent a letter requesting the county government to issue registration forms for temples as a reference to proceed with the programs.

Temporary Suspension of Posthumous Bone Cleansing for Ms. Han. In Wu County Local Court, regarding the exhumation and bone‑boiling in the case of Ms. Han—whose body has not yet decomposed—and given that her stomach and intestines have been sent to Shanghai for toxicological examination, the court has decided to suspend the bone‑cleansing process for now.

Wuxi

County Administrative Council Preparatory Meeting Minutes. The county government will hold its first County Administrative Council meeting for three days starting February 20. Yesterday at 2 pm they convened a preparatory session and resolved: (1) proposals must be submitted by 6 pm on February 20; (2) establish five review committees—Group 1: Education; Group 2: Public Security; Group 3: Construction and Industry; Group 4: Self‑Government and Public Welfare; Group 5: Finance; (3) each committee will have three to five members, with Groups 1 and 5 having three and the others five; (4) appointed members are: Group 1—Gu Zhuo, Gao Yang, Lu Renshou; Group 2—Huang Zhenbai, Yang Shoumei, Hua Changshou, Jiang Zumin, Su Wenbin; Group 3—Rong Desheng, Chen Zhanru, Wu Bangjie, Yao Dixin, Lu Qi; Group 5—Wu Bangzhou, Shen Weixin, Zhu Chenghong, Qian Zhongliang, Chen Zhanru; (5) Magistrate Sun will serve ex officio as chairman, with Xu Yisong proposed as vice‑chairman. The meeting adjourned. Today (20th) is the first official day, with an opening ceremony at 9 am in the county government auditorium, and the formal session will begin this afternoon.

Suspected Fence Voluntarily Surrenders. In the administrative jurisdiction’s Huai Shi Market, at Mr. You Jisheng’s home in Chen Lane, thieves Ma Fushou, Qian Ada, and two others were captured last winter by Political‑Public Security Officer Tang Yushun. They confessed to the theft and were each sentenced to life imprisonment. Recently, You Jisheng’s nephew, You Genrong, reported to the Seventh District office that on the 13th Tang Yushun and Chen Ada summoned him to the home of local protector Qian Xubao and extorted 300 yuan, later reduced to 60 yuan to be paid within 20 days. District Chief Zhao immediately reported the matter to the county government. The county summoned You Genrong, Tang Yushun, Chen Ada, and Qian Xubao for questioning. At yesterday’s hearing, You Genrong reiterated his claim. Tang Yushun contended that Genrong had masterminded the original theft, recruiting Ma Fushou and Qian Ada to seize the 3,000 yuan hidden under the floorboards, and fearing they would expose him, voluntarily gave them 50 yuan without coercion. Qian Xubao confirmed that Genrong sent 50 yuan afterward and warned them not to speak. After verification, the court ordered Genrong detained, while Tang Yushun and the others were released on bail pending further investigation.

River Transport Representatives from Jiangsu Petition in Beijing. Faced with the Ministry of Construction’s plan to levy a new inland‑river packet‑boat river‑management fee, the river transport guilds of Jiangsu counties held joint meetings and resolved to send representatives to petition the central ministries and provincial government. Although the Ministry of Communications initially approved withdrawing the plan, the provincial government—citing its prior Provincial Affairs Council decision and the urgent need to dredge waterways—reinstated the levy effective January 1. Local operators vehemently opposed this and resolved to dispatch representatives to the Executive Yuan. On the 19th, six delegates from Nanjing, Shanghai, Wuxi, Zhenjiang, Nantong, and Wujin arrived in Nanjing, assembled at the Nanjing River Transport Guild, and at 10 am presented their petition to Secretary‑General Liu Yongkai at the Executive Yuan. Liu acknowledged their hardships, said he would forward the petition to the Premier, and instruct the Ministry of Communications and the provincial government to handle it impartially. The delegates expressed satisfaction and departed.

Civil Affairs Department Section Chief Goes to Province. Qian Sunqing, chairman of the Wuxi County Chamber of Commerce and of the River Transport Guild, was recently appointed by the Jiangsu provincial government as Section Chief of the First Section of the Civil Affairs Department. Qian is scheduled to arrive at the department on the 21st to assume his post.

Changzhou 79

Establishment of Relief Home Organization. In Wuyi, the county government submitted plans for a relief home to the Civil Affairs Department, which ordered immediate preparation. County Magistrate Zhu yesterday announced the formal establishment of the institution, appointing Feng Xiaoqing as director and Shen Pengnian as deputy director.

Police Raid Gambling Den. On the afternoon of the 18th, under county magistrate’s orders, Police Captain Chen Gang of Wuyi led a 26‑officer team by boat to the Sanxing Teahouse at Qishuyan to crack down on gambling. Having been tipped off, the gambling suspects had already scattered. The owner Chen Bujin and partner Wu Shousheng were found packing up marked mahjong tiles, playing cards, dominoes, and gambling funds. Captain Chen seized these and arrested Wu Shousheng and others, delivering them to the county on the 19th for questioning.

Jiangyin 80

Interrogation in the Hongxingsha Assassination Case. North of town at Hongxingsha, a man surnamed Tan was shot to death. Following autopsy, with the killer at large, County Police Chief Jin Chuyun was ordered to investigate. On the 18th, they arrested Yun Hongzhang of Yangjiaqiao, Jingjiang, suspected of involvement, and brought him in for questioning. Yun said he now works at a lace factory on Gordon Road in Shanghai and knew Tan from school. He had planned to return to Jingjiang on the 17th but learned aboard the boat at Jiangyin from Wang Yongcai of Yitai Clothing Store that Tan was dead, allegedly killed by Cai Yihua, and that Tan’s fiancée might be implicated. Witnesses including Tan’s parents, store partner Wang Yongcai, matchmaker Ji Dongshi, brother‑in‑law Bao Xingcai, mother‑in‑law Bao Shi, and fiancée Tan Bao all gave conflicting and bizarre accounts demanding justice. The court ordered Yun Hongzhang, Bao Xingcai, Bao Shi, and Ji Dongshi each held separately pending further investigation.

Uncertain Death of Fan Taoyuan. Within ten days, two homicides occurred in District Two at Wenqiao Town’s Dayuandi. Fan Taoyuan, married to Zhang Xiaomei but also involved with another woman, Zhang Wenbao, married the latter’s niece on the 14th day of the lunar New Year. Enraged, Zhang Xiaomei confronted him and had three acres of his land seized. Only then did Fan calm down. On the morning of the 18th, his body was found hanging from a small tree behind the back gate of Wang Ziting. Close examination showed a thick rope and blood on the knees, indicating the body had been moved postmortem. Wang Ziting reported this to the county for autopsy.

Zhenjiang 81

Villagers Petition to Stop Competition from Yao’s Boat Service. In Shunjiang Township, local merchants Wu Meiqiao, Wang Xiaobo, and others, running the Su–Datong river route, competed so fiercely that the Benniu steamer boiler exploded, causing unprecedented disaster. Realizing the danger, the companies briefly ceased operations—but soon Datong, Litong, and Yongda each launched services simultaneously, cutting fares and increasing speed to prove superiority. Fearing a repeat of the Benniu tragedy, yesterday the county wrote to the River Transport Guild urging humane regulation and an even distribution of daily departures to avoid risk.

Provincial Police Corps Reassigns Commanders. The Jiangsu Provincial Police Corps has reassigned its artillery police leadership: Captain Wang Xuehong of Artillery Police First Unit has been replaced by Second Lieutenant Inspector Ding Tingcan of the Second Unit. Additionally, Major Xiong Zhaodong, fallen in action at Rugao, is succeeded by Second Lieutenant Inspector He Songlin of the Third Brigade, Eighth Company. They officially exchanged posts yesterday.

Gaoyou 82

Troops Arrive in Gaoyou for Garrison Duty. The 266th Regiment of the 45th Division of the National Army arrived in Gaoyou yesterday. The regimental headquarters and direct units are stationed at Shanyin Temple; the third battalion at Chengtian Temple; the first company at the Confucian Temple; and the fourth company at Tianwang Temple.

Bandits Rob and Kill in Honglianzhuang. In Honglianzhuang village the night before last around 8 pm, twenty to thirty bandits armed with firearms raided homes, searching every house and executing farmers Meng Weihui and Meng Weiru. They made off with over a thousand yuan.

Rugao 83

Provincial Police Force Deployed to Rugao. On the 14th, the Provincial Police Corps headquarters relocated to Haian, and the First and Second Brigades have successively deployed to Rugao, garrisoning Rugao Middle School.

New Public Security Chief Arrives in Rugao. County Public Security Chief Shen Jinghua has been transferred to Shanghai. His replacement, Ma Guochao, formerly head of Jintan County Public Security, arrived in Rugao on the 15th and will assume his post shortly.

River Police Repel Bandits. In border areas such as Niantou Port, bandits often lurk. On the 14th, River Police patrol vessel No. 4 docked at the port. After nightfall, bandits probed with flashlights. The vessel’s police disembarked, challenged the group in the darkness, and, receiving no password response, fired two warning shots. The bandits returned fire with box guns. The River Police lay prone and returned fire, exchanging shots for over ten minutes before routing the bandits.

Two Bandits Executed. On the 12th at Shizhuang, under orders from Qing Township HQ, the county police Third Company executed bandits Geng Zhengyuan and Wu Kun’er (also known as Wu Zikeng), publicly announcing their crimes.

Capital Punishment and Head Display as Warning. On the 10th, the Third Company of the county police, responding to a report that bandits in Shizhuang were demanding 1,000 yuan from villagers, rushed there. The bandits were extorting village head Yu Yushu; they were immediately apprehended, and one who attempted to flee was executed on the spot and his head displayed as a warning to the public.

Nantong 84

Remaining Western District Communists Rob Weapons from Police. On the night of the 18th, over two hundred Communist bandits armed with box guns and machine guns surrounded the West District police station, seizing weapons and ammunition. Policeman Wu Zhenqing was kidnapped, and the shops of merchants Chen Wanfeng and Zheng Wanxing were looted. Reactionary slogans were pasted in the market. On the 19th, County Magistrate Zhan went to inspect the scene.

Inquiry into Hit‑and‑Run Case Involving American. The Ministry of Foreign Affairs telegraphed the Nantong County Government to request details of the case in which American Schmidt, driving an automobile in Nantong, fatally struck a Chinese pedestrian. County Magistrate Chen immediately submitted a report.

Telegrams to Coordinate Crackdown on Border‑Area Bandits. At the Nantong–Rugao county boundary, bandits and Communists run rampant and are spreading to neighboring districts. The situation is urgent. County Magistrate Chen telegraphed Rugao Magistrate Liu to jointly decide on the date for a suppression operation.

Holding a Countywide Anti‑Bandit Conference. On the 19th a Countywide Anti‑Bandit Conference was held in Tongxi, resolving: (1) allocate 8,440 yuan for suppression expenses; (2) each sub‑bureau must be established by March 1; (3) committee members to be proposed by district chiefs and approved by the county government; (4) the review board will hold regular meetings twice a month.

Xiaoshan 85

Joint Three‑County Police Operation Against Bandits. In Tianle Township at the junction of the three counties, bandits ran rampant. Police forces from all three counties and the public security police cooperated in a joint operation to eradicate them.

Local Woman Attempts Suicide Over Broken Romance. Ms. Shi of Zhujiaqiao, Xiaoshan—formerly a student in Hangzhou—fell in love last year with Mr. Shen of Shaoxing. In January of this year, Shen suddenly married another woman. Upon hearing this, Ms. Shi attempted to drown herself in brine but was noticed by bystanders and sent to Hangzhou Daxing Hospital for treatment; her survival remains uncertain.

Shortfall in Education Funding to Be Supplemented. Xiaoshan’s education budget has long been insufficient. With the expansion of rural primary schools, higher primary schools, and vocational education, the new budget is short by several ten‑thousand yuan. The magistrate proposed that, effective this year, an additional four jiao per liang be levied—a proposal approved by the provincial finance departments.

Shangyu 86

In the case of fraud involving the May Thirtieth Massacre relief funds: the relief payments for the families of victims of the May Thirtieth Massacre, after years of negotiation by the Support Association, were only resolved at the end of last year. One of the townsmen who perished then was Shi Songs­heng; his mother was instructed by the Association at the end of the lunar year to travel to Shanghai to receive two thousand yuan in relief. Upon arriving and unfamiliar with the city, she applied to the Shangyu Association in Shanghai for assistance. The Association dispatched staff member Zhang Jieshou to accompany her. Under the Association’s name, a receipt was signed by Shi’s mother to collect the funds. However, Zhang immediately deducted 150 yuan for travel expenses, 200 yuan for entertainment, and over 1,600 yuan for other charges, leaving Shi Songs­heng’s mother with only 300 yuan. Swallowing her indignation, she returned to Shangyu. She now intends to sue Zhang for fraud in the county government and has sent a petition to the Shanghai District Court requesting his arrest, severe punishment, and restitution of the misappropriated funds.

A tragic episode of irresponsible romance: Xu Shangchuan, former board member of Donghai Primary School in Shangxu Village, Dongxiang, last spring, driven by rampant banditry, hid his young son in the school. The boy’s sleeping quarters were in the same room as teacher Shen Changyan. Since Shen routinely left early and returned late, this arrangement was taken for granted. The boy, barely toddling, needed help each morning dressing, a task performed by his sister Qiuj­u (aged fifteen and a student at the school). Before long, she was seduced by Changyan and became pregnant, her abdomen swelling over time. Fearing ridicule, she continued to wear a lined cheongsam in the bitter winter to conceal her condition. When the winter vacation ended, Changyan, fearing implication, made an excuse and silently fled, disappearing without a trace. On the night of the lunar calendar’s twelfth month, twenty‑eighth, Qiuj­u was seized by severe labor pains. Her parents and sister‑in‑law, enraged, refused to let her give birth at home and forced her outdoors to deliver in the open air amid heavy snowfall. She cried out in agony; nearby villagers covered their ears and dared not approach. She gave birth to a son, then collapsed. The newborn froze to death in the cold. Though villagers attempted to aid Qiuj­u, the ordeal was too great; she died the next morning. When this matter came to the attention of the education authorities, they were outraged and intend to issue a decree to all county schools that Shen Changyan shall never again be employed.

Education News and Highlights

Ministry of Education Issues by Order 87

General Procedures for the Organization of Social Organizations

By order of the Executive Yuan, the Ministry of Education hereby circulates the General Procedures for the Organization of Social Organizations to all provincial education departments, education bureaus in all special municipalities, all directly governed universities, all approved private universities, and the National Peking Library Antiquities Preservation Office. According to Executive Yuan Instruction No. 550, and in compliance with National Government Instruction No. 72, and further by correspondence from the Central Executive Committee: the People’s Organizations Plan passed at the Second Plenary Session clearly divides people’s organizations into professional organizations and social organizations. The procedures for organizing professional organizations are explicitly defined in the Plan, whereas for social organizations the original draft only stipulates that they be organized under the Party’s guidance and government supervision. Detailed organizational procedures urgently need to be supplemented to provide clear guidance. Having been approved at the 70th Standing Meeting, the Social Organization Procedures are hereby issued; all Party committees at every level are to comply. A full text is enclosed for reference and for notification to all subordinate bodies.

A copy of the Social Organization Procedures is hereby distributed for immediate compliance. In addition to issuing this circular and separate orders, the original Organization Procedures Order shall also be distributed for full awareness, and subordinate bodies shall be instructed accordingly.

A copy of the Social Organization Procedures is hereby distributed for immediate compliance. In addition to issuing this circular and separate orders, the original Organization Procedures Order shall also be distributed for full awareness, and subordinate bodies shall be instructed accordingly.

A copy of the Social Organization Procedures is hereby distributed for immediate compliance. In addition to issuing this circular and separate orders, the original Organization Procedures Order shall also be distributed for full awareness, and subordinate bodies shall be instructed accordingly.

National Education Conference: Xinjiang Province Appoints Wang Ruyi as Delegate 88

Yesterday (the 19th), the Ministry of Education received a telegram from the Xinjiang Provincial Education Department stating that the province has appointed Wang Ruyi, of its Beijing Liaison Office, as its representative to attend the National Education Conference. The Ministry has since formally invited Mr. Wang to attend the meeting at the appointed time.

Yu Wencan Resigns as Director of the General Affairs Department of the Ministry of Education; Reassigned as President of the Peking Taxation School 89

General Affairs Department Director Yu Wencan has been transferred to serve as President of the Peking Taxation School and has tendered his resignation from the Director post, approved by Minister Chiang. Deputy Director of the Ministry’s General Affairs Department, Jiang Shaomo, is appointed acting Director. Yesterday (the 19th) afternoon, all staff of the General Affairs Department held a farewell meeting. Mr. Yu departed by the Pupu train bound for Peking.

Ministry of Education Commends Hu Wentu for Donating 10,000 Yuan to Daxia University 90

Mr. Hu Wentu of Singapore, a leading figure among Nanyang overseas Chinese known for his long‑standing public spirit, last summer assisted in fundraising for new buildings at Shanghai Daxia University during its tour of British colonial ports in the Nanyang. Mr. Hu’s efforts were exemplary, and he generously donated 10,000 yuan in national currency to the university. Upon the university’s request, the Ministry of Education has ordered commendation to promote this benevolent act. The Ministry’s directive states: “In reviewing the case, it is confirmed that overseas Chinese Mr. Hu Wentu donated 10,000 yuan toward the construction of the university. According to the Regulations on Commending Donations for Educational Enterprise, he should be granted a First‑Class Certificate of Commendation to encourage others. Besides publication in this Ministry’s Gazette, one First‑Class Certificate of Commendation shall be issued immediately.” The certificate has been forwarded by the university to Mr. Hu.

Jiangsu Education Department Issues Order to Convene Meeting of County Education Bureau Directors 91

The Jiangsu Provincial Education Department has issued an order to all county education bureaus: in order to assess the quality and efficiency of local education administration, which crucially shapes the future of education, and to solicit opinions for improvement, a temporary meeting of all county education bureau directors shall be convened in accordance with the “Provisional Regulations for the Meetings of County Education Bureau Directors of Jiangsu Province,” consisting of thirteen articles. This order is issued concurrently to the Jiangsu Provincial Government’s Education Office for record, with the meeting date to be announced separately. All bureau directors must prepare detailed reports on their jurisdictions for consideration.

Provisional Regulations for the Meetings of County Education Bureau Directors of Jiangsu Province

Article 1. The purpose of these meetings is to improve local education and enhance administrative efficiency.

Article 2. These meetings shall be convened periodically by the Jiangsu Provincial Education Department, with the list of counties to be summoned determined for each session.

Article 3. All duly summoned county education bureau directors must attend; in case of valid impediment, the county superintendent may attend as a substitute.

Article 4. The Jiangsu Provincial Education Department Director shall preside as Chair; if the Chair is absent, one of the attending members may be appointed to preside.

Article 5. The Department’s Section Chiefs, Superintendents, and other relevant staff may attend by designation of the Department Director.

Article 6. Proposals for discussion shall be submitted by the Department Director.

Article 7. If any county director has a proposal, it must be submitted to the Jiangsu Provincial Education Department for review and inclusion at least two weeks before the meeting.

Article 8. Any proposals requiring special review shall be assigned by the Chair to a designated committee of attending members.

Article 9. Decisions of the meeting shall be implemented by the Jiangsu Provincial Education Department as appropriate.

Article 10. Each meeting shall be limited to five days; extensions may be granted if there are excessive agenda items.

Article 11. Travel and lodging expenses for county directors attending the meeting shall be borne by their respective county education bureaus.

Article 12. Meeting procedures shall be separately prescribed by the Jiangsu Provincial Education Department.

Article 13. These regulations shall take effect upon promulgation by the Jiangsu Provincial Education Department and shall be filed for record with the Ministry of Education and the Jiangsu Provincial Government.

Central Academy Excavates the Yin Ruins at Anyang; National Government Orders Henan Province to Heighten Protection 92

(Central News) Yesterday, to ensure the protection of the Central Academy’s excavation work at the Yin ruins in Anyang, the National Government issued a special instruction to the Henan Provincial Government. According to a report by Cai Yuanpei, President of the National Central Academy, concerning the excavation methods for the Anyang Yin ruins, approval is requested for the proposed arrangements. The Henan Provincial Government is directed to pay close attention to safeguarding the Academy’s work.

Previously, the Academy had been excavating the ancient Yin Dynasty site in Anyang County, Henan, with the Province’s gracious consent for over a year, yielding significant results. However, in October of last year, He Richang, Director of the Henan Provincial Library, claimed under provincial orders the right to excavate, issuing notices banning other parties from excavation, causing the Academy’s work to be suspended. The Academy, focusing on scholarly rather than proprietary aims, sees no precedent for such conflict. Two years of preparation have secured needed experts and modern archaeological tools, attracting domestic and foreign scholars who regard the site as a new epoch in Chinese antiquarian studies. The Academy is confident it will not disturb stratigraphy or destroy historical remains and feels a strong sense of responsibility. On October 30 last year, it petitioned the National Government to oversee the work; the Chairman approved and ordered the Henan Provincial Government to resume protection of the Central Academy’s excavation, and to halt any unauthorized activities by He Richang. Concurrently, the Academy dispatched its Institute of History and Philology Director, Chuan Si, to negotiate in person, emphasizing sincere consultation. Thanks to the Chairman’s oversight and in the spirit of harmony between central academic bodies and local authorities for the advancement of scholarship, Chuan Si won the sympathy of local dignitaries. After five weeks, on December 28, the Henan Provincial Government issued five measures for resolution, hereby submitted for your review. If approved by Your Excellency, we respectfully request authorization and formation of the record, and that the Henan Provincial Government be notified.

Ministry of Education Administrative Plan for January, February, and March (I) 93

The Ministry of Education has approved and submitted for record its administrative plan for the third quarter of the eighteenth academic year (January through March of the nineteenth year). The original plan text is as follows:

Regarding the Promulgation and Implementation of Various Regulations for Universities and Technical Schools

1. Issue a general order requiring public and private universities and technical schools to submit organizational structure outlines, and urge them to amend their practices in accordance with organizational laws and regulations.

2. Issue a general order to survey tuition‑free education policies at public and private universities and technical schools, and to formulate general rules on tuition‑free education for enforcement by all universities.

3. Issue a general order requiring all student associations at public and private universities and technical schools to reorganize from the next semester onwards in compliance with the Student Self‑Government Association Organizational Outline promulgated by the central authorities.

4. Issue a general order requiring public and private universities and technical schools, from the next semester onward, to apply in advance for examination committee members before the graduation examination period, in accordance with regulations.

Regarding the Review of Various Reports from Universities and Technical Schools

1. Review the reports on faculty and new student enrollments at public and private universities and technical schools for the eighteenth academic year.

2. Review reports on character training and military training implementation at public and private universities and technical schools.

3. Review provincial and special municipal government responses concerning the establishment of technical schools in their localities.

4. Review status reports from research institutes or research centers at various universities.

Regarding the Compilation of Higher Education General Statistics

1. Compile the seventeenth academic year status statistics for all public and private universities and technical schools nationwide.

2. Compile graduation statistics for all universities and specialized schools nationwide.

3. Compile current statistics on students studying abroad.

4. Issue a general order requiring registered academic societies to report their present status for academic statistical compilation.

Regarding the Development of Curriculum Standards for Universities and Technical Schools

1. Organize and collate collected curriculum materials from domestic and foreign universities and technical schools to inform the drafting of curriculum standards.

2. Establish drafting committees for curriculum standards in arts, sciences, law, and medicine at universities.

Regarding Inspection and Regulation of Private Universities and Technical Schools

1. Inspect private universities, colleges, and technical schools applying for establishment.

2. Issue a general order that private universities, colleges, and technical schools founded after February 1 shall not enroll students without prior approval.

3. Continue to close down unsuitable private universities, colleges, and technical schools.

Regarding the Development and Implementation of Regulations for Students Studying Abroad

1. Draft plans for preparatory schools for overseas study.

2. Implement registration procedures for students studying abroad.

3. Promulgate regulations for the selection and dispatch of government‑funded overseas students.

Regarding the Preparation of the National Education Conference

1. Draft various regulations for the National Education Conference.

2. Establish the National Education Conference Preparatory Committee.

3. Issue general orders instructing all provinces, special municipalities, and national universities to send delegates to the conference.

4. Select experts for various conference tasks.

5. Arrange venues and hospitality for the conference.

6. Expedite the drafting of agenda items.

Regarding the Compilation of Education Schemes

1. Submit two reviewed schemes—for compulsory education and adult evening education—to the central authorities for approval.

2. Submit completed draft schemes for teacher training, elementary education, secondary education, and higher education for review.

3. Expedite unfinished schemes for social education, Mongolian and Tibetan education, overseas Chinese education, Party ideological education, and education administration.

Regarding the Formulation of Physical Education Standards for Primary and Secondary Schools

1. Analyze various in‑class and extracurricular activities of primary and secondary students.

2. Collate and compile collected materials on physical education in primary and secondary schools.

3. Gather reference materials on physical education from Western primary and secondary schools.

4. Draft physical education standards for primary and secondary schools.

Regarding the Formulation of Curriculum Standards

1. Continue revising the curriculum for high school teacher training departments.

2. Continue revising high school curricula in agriculture, industry, and commerce.

Regarding Overseas Chinese Education

1. Complete the overseas Chinese education scheme.

2. Compile various forms required for the registration of overseas Chinese schools.

3. Handle matters related to the registration of overseas Chinese schools.

Regarding the Evaluation of Provincial and Municipal Education Administration

1. Review work reports from provincial and municipal education administrative bodies.

2. Review the most recent three‑month work plans submitted by provincial and municipal education administrative bodies.

3. Review matters submitted by provincial and municipal education administrative bodies.

Shanghai Education Bureau Reorganizes Physical Education School Curriculum 94

Zhengfeng Literary Institute to Build Its Own Campus 97

Zhengfeng Literary Institute, located at 345 Jing’an Temple Road, has been operating for over two years as a specialized academy for classical Chinese studies. This term it has added eminent literature professors such as Hu Puan, Yao Minghui, Zhu Xiangwan, Zhang Yanchang, Qi Fanniu, and Bao Dujiu. Enrollment has surged. Last year the Institute secured nine acres of vacant land at the end of North Sichuan Road near Gaotalu Road. Terms have been agreed, and new campus construction will commence this year, with completion expected by the autumn term. The currently rented premises will serve only as temporary classrooms. Plans for formal registration will be submitted to the Ministry once construction is completed.

Sports

Funds for Participation in the Far Eastern Championship: Chairman Chiang Instructs the Executive Yuan to Handle 98

The Ninth Far Eastern Championship Games are scheduled for May 24 to 31 in Tokyo, Japan. Both the Japanese and Philippine governments have allocated substantial funds, making significant preparations to secure victory and international prestige. Our country, however, currently has no funding sources for participation. The governing body, the All‑China Sports Promotion Association, previously resolved at a board meeting that the standard requirement is eighty thousand yuan, of which forty thousand yuan shall be raised by the Association and forty thousand yuan requested from the National Government, to bolster morale. We also request that the Government appoint a representative to attend the Games. The Association has received a reply from the Government’s Secretariat indicating that Chairman Chiang has instructed the Executive Yuan to handle the matter. The original official letter reads: “To the All‑China Sports Promotion Association: Your request for Government subsidy of forty thousand yuan for participation in the Ninth Far Eastern Championship Games in Tokyo from May 24 and for the dispatch of Government representatives has been referred to the Executive Yuan for approval.” Considering that when the Games were held in our country, the Japanese and Philippine governments each contributed one hundred thousand yuan and even dispatched warships as escorts, demonstrating meticulous attention to international sports, our request of only forty thousand yuan is modest. The Government is expected to grant our application to enable our delegation to proceed.

Medals of the Citywide Sports Meet 99

For the Shanghai Special Municipality’s citywide sports meet, in addition to soliciting prizes from all sectors, a specially designed medal has been created featuring an eagle clutching a cup in its beak, symbolizing heroic victory. This model was produced by sculptor Mr. Brans, introduced to Shanghai by expatriate artist Mr. Philip, who came from Belgium. Brans is one of the foremost figures in modern art; he once crafted portraits of the Belgian crown prince’s newly married couple and received a special honor. While in Shanghai, he is staying at the Bund’s Sassoon House and will soon depart for Japan. Keen to leave a work in China as a memento, he thus created this unique medal for the sports meet, sure to invigorate the athletes’ spirits.

Free Talk

Take It with a Laugh 106

Wife: “My love, I’ve just found a letter you sent me before we were married. It has a line that says, ‘Even if a gentle breeze brushes against your face, I shall be jealous, because it has caressed your lovely cheeks.’ How sweet that is.”

Husband: “Oh? That’s because every time I come see you you open the window wide, and I can’t stand the cold breeze—so I wrote that.”

Student A: “You’ve written three letters asking your father for money. Have you received a reply?”

Student B: “Not yet. Today I sent a fourth, saying, ‘Your son has frozen to death. Please remit two or three hundred gold taels immediately for funeral expenses.’”

Company Manager (to his newly appointed chief accountant): “Your father has such distinguished bearing. His snow‑white beard and hair look splendid.”

Chief Accountant: “He should thank me. Last time at a tax office, I skimmed off a huge sum of public funds, which turned his hair white overnight.”

Girl A: “He proposed to me yesterday. I don’t wish to marry him, but he’s quite pitiful. What gentle words should I use to refuse him?”

Girl B: “Just tell him your father is short of funds and you have no money.”

Anecdotes of Cao Zhuochao 107

Cao Zhuochao, given name Jiada, style name Yingfu, was a native of Jiangyin. By nature he was indolent and unskilled at household management or production. Reading Wang Wenduan’s poetry, he could not believe the line that cuckoos are inept at building nests much like their speech, sighed, “These words fit me,” and adopted “Zhuochao” as his pseudonym. In youth he studied with Wu Maocai at Menggeng You and began writing poetry, though with little distinction. After enrolling in the academy, he studied under Chen Zifu for twelve years, diligently preparing for the imperial examinations. When Qin Shaoling served as prefectural instructor of Jiading, he praised Cao’s talent and discussed classics and histories with him day and night, finally revealing Cao’s promise. In the renyin year Cao was recommended as Filial and Incorrupt and reported to Shaoling, who said, “With your talent and learning, I knew you would be chosen, yet your outspoken nature often breaks taboos—what can be done?” Soon Shaoling left Jiangyin and Cao waited in vain. Later appointed magistrate in Shandong, Cao found no fit and returned home, just as national upheaval began.

Cao was thin and unkempt, his garments and sleeves stained with ink. Honest to a fault, he would not conceal anyone’s error even before a crowded hall; some resented him but none dared act. With classmates Chu Huachou, Zhang Xiongbo, Jiang Zhuzhuang, Jin Songcen, Ding Zhonghu, and others, all nicknamed him “Cao the Simple.” Expert in all studies, he excelled especially in poetry. After the turmoil, he let his hair grow long and did not shave, lodging in Yunjian and teaching to support himself, never speaking of worldly affairs. With no food stored for the night, he would still chant amid wind and rain, his voice resonant as metal and stone, delighting in nature’s inspiration. Skilled in short lyrics, his clarity approached that of the Daoist Bai. In the winter of guihai, when the author and Cao walked west of the city to observe ancient trees and lone crows, upon their return Cao filled an entire “A River Red” lyric by lamplight. The author had completed his first draft, which included the line, “Near dusk, the remnant sunlight threads through like tattered silk,” adding a sobbing note. Cao said, “That makes me put down my pen,” and never finished the piece.

Cao also excelled in ink‑plum paintings: petals fragrant as winter’s heart, branches slender and elegant, delicate and graceful. Sometimes three years passed without a work; other times he painted dozens in a single day. None could fathom his depths. Normally he disliked wine, yet a little banana‑leaf brew soon made him tipsy. When drunk he danced and sang Kunqu. At his seat, arrangements of tile jars and glass bottles formed a constellation; he plucked cassia blossoms and pickled lemons and roses, concocting every taste from pungent to sweet to sour. To guests he’d say, “This is my Guanfusi wine; this is Boshi’s wine,” and pour, “This is what I fancy,” for his friend loved pungency, he loved sweetness, and Cao himself delighted in sour. He adored flowers, planting them in every crack of his small courtyard. He often led his Guanfusi friend to the countryside to gather wild chrysanthemums among ancient graves, calling it pleasure. He also hunted edible greens with a small knife and wicker basket, crawling along muddy paths without care for stains. His wild abandon was unlike ordinary folk. Though many acquaintances were eminent, he never sought them. When teased, he’d frown, “Had I bent my integrity even a little, I’d hold high office long ago. Why remain in squalor? Better to starve in a ditch; I am pleased to wake with dew and sleep under stars.”

He exchanged verses with Wu Changshuo and Junqing; it was said no one understood Cao’s depths more than Guanfusi Boshi.

Bits of Common Knowledge 108

— Tang Bihua

• Getting up early to eat noodles at a noodle shop is the best deal, because morning servings are more generous at any time. After nine o’clock, portions shrink considerably. Don’t believe it? Try it once.

• On a rainy day, when a car splashes mud as it passes, hold your umbrella handle sideways and tilt the canopy to block the spray—then the mud will strike the umbrella instead.

• When asking friends outside town to send you a money order via the post office, have them specify the nearest branch on the order—e.g. “___ Road Post Office.” Collecting it then becomes much more convenient. Otherwise, whether nearby or far, you must always go to the main post office, which is a great hassle.

Interesting Notes on Mahjong 109

— Yan Ye

Mahjong has become a national pastime. In cities, everyone plays; even illiterate villagers know how to “zhong‑fa‑dong‑xi.” The other day I discussed mahjong with a friend and noted some amusing stories; here are a couple.

In a certain part of Pudong, the “Two of Myriads” tile is commonly called “Minhua.” The story goes that at the end of the Qing, a man named Minrong was extremely poor until he unexpectedly discovered twenty thousand in a buried cache, instantly becoming wealthy. With his riches but no status, he felt like a man wearing brocade in the dark. He then spent eight thousand over six months to build a grand mansion, earning villagers’ envy. Still lacking rank, he donated a huge sum to secure an official post, hung banners at his gate, and lived richly with servants galore. But fortunes reversed with the Wuchang Uprising: his donations were confiscated, his rank was lost, and after years of waste his money ran out—he was once again poor and lowly. Those now thirty have all witnessed this, so spending twenty thousand was once so easy for him that players call the “Two of Myriads” tile “Minhua.” Everyone knows “Minrong” means twenty thousand. Alas, Minrong’s story remains a grand tale.

Years ago at the Jiangsu Provincial Second Normal School, a beautiful English teacher once told her students in class, “Your country’s mahjong has its organization and variety; I enjoy it greatly. Do many of you excel at it?” The students replied, “Mahjong is a game of the lower classes; the educated disdain it.” She retorted, “I don’t believe that. I often see your high officials, wealthy magnates, teachers, and students all enjoy it. Are they all from the lower classes? Then your entire people must be lowly.” Her remark silenced them.

In a tea house in Dongting Town, Wuxi, a mahjong league chanted a verse:

“After one hand’s done, with chow, pung, and kong align

Three Dragons hit—just draw the last tile divine

Before eight rounds end, losing the meld yields one pure suit

Then on the Kong you draw Blossom—what a loot!”

Mahjong enthusiasts hearing this must itch to join in.

A certain county magistrate rigorously banned gambling, earning widespread praise. One night he traveled to Beijing, and friends arranged a late mahjong session for him. Suddenly police burst in and arrested everyone. The magistrate hastily gave a false name, claiming to be a merchant. Because their attire was fine, the police fined them a hundred gold taels. The magistrate paid and was released. People joked that the man who once fined others for gambling was now arrested and fined himself—what goes around comes around.

Record of the New Dormitory at Jiaotong University 110

— Zhi Zheng

At Shanghai West Jiaotong University, since the tenures of Presidents Sun and Li, school affairs have advanced considerably. Last year the administration, seeing student housing inadequate, commenced a new dormitory. After six months of work, it was completed this spring. Its exquisite facilities and distinctive architecture merit description.

The new dormitory sits behind the gymnasium, on land acquired under President Ling Zhuoming. The U‑shaped, three‑story building contains over one hundred rooms. On the ground floor are two large halls: one a reading room with an extensive collection of books and newspapers, the other a common room for gatherings, both tastefully arranged. Two students share each room, which includes a Western‑style wooden wardrobe and, in the left corner, a full‑height mirror for dressing. Matching desks, chairs, and bookshelves—purchased from XinXin Company—accompany new‑style spring‑mattress beds that are both soft and firm. The walls are painted light yellow, the ceilings white. Each room features a large window, providing excellent air and light. Students eagerly moved in and vied to decorate: some hung colored paintings, others calligraphy screens, blending Chinese and Western artistic elements. The washrooms have both hot and cold water piping, and the toilets are modeled after European style—standing inside feels like entering a grand hotel. Among domestic universities, this dormitory likely ranks first. In front, a broad avenue circles the building and leads directly off campus, though paving is not yet complete. Outside the dorm is a plaza bordered by cypress groves—air quality is superb and rarely matched. We hear that during the spring break the school will hold an official opening ceremony and invite the public to visit, which promises to be a grand occasion.

More Notes on Being Afraid of One’s Wife 111

— Meng Qi

I recently read Ms. Lanxiu’s notes on “fear of one’s wife” in this column. Although a minor issue, it holds research value and a certain mystery. I will also discuss it here.

Many believe that, by principles of gender equality, a husband should not fear his wife—if she becomes unreasonable, he can handle her. This view is wrong. The fear arises from love; it differs entirely from fearing parents or teachers. Because of love, one indulges everything, accepting all her words and demands, whether right or wrong, reaching the point of genuine fear.

Others say it matters not: women are narrow‑minded and earnest. If their wishes are thwarted, they cry and disturb household harmony; if illness follows, the loss is greater. Better to yield. This view has merit, yet its root remains love.

Friends newly married or engaged say that fearing one’s wife is a delightful comedy. Imagine your beloved demands something—no matter how unreasonable—her expression enchants you. If you refuse, she pouts and cries, and you will, in pain, relent. Though near farce, it has true flavor. Such fear is surely an expression of love.

From these points we conclude that any man who fears his wife must love her deeply. I hope everyone fears their wife.

As for results: some men chafe under strict domestic rules and find it burdensome. But I believe a man who fears his wife has fewer chances to sin, spends less money, preserves his morals, and improves his temper. Don’t believe me? Consider American novelist Washington Irving’s famous line: “Men who are courteous and kind to everyone outside the home have surely endured a shrew at home.” Isn’t it so?

Heartfelt Warmth (No. 133) 112

In the ninth installment, famed scholars play hide‑and‑seek, and a fair maiden freely offers embraces.

Moreover, this not only unsettles women’s psychology but contradicts the public’s expectations. From grand political matters to trifles, we always yearn for fresh novelties. Women’s fashions also change continually: clothes made in the first half of the year often fall out of favor by the second, sometimes beyond alteration, forcing a return to ancient styles. Now the eldest aunt opens a trunk full of garments left by elder sister Ruiyun—some look as if just cut and sewn, yet in both style and pattern they belong to a bygone era. Poor clothes—like a person who has never stepped into the limelight, already outdated. The aunt tried to coax Ruiyun into wearing them, praising their cost. Ruiyun knew it true but, performing daily onstage, felt embarrassed seeing others in colorful costumes while she wore antiques. At fifteen, keen to outshine others, she naturally grew gloomy. Eventually Ajin said, “Today so many girls perform onstage. You need a few good outfits. That style isn’t in fashion now.” The aunt had no choice but to agree: “Tomorrow Ajin will take you to the old tailors on Laojiefu Street for fittings.” Now as a young master, one must yield—better to secure top clothing for the future. Ajin accompanied her, fitting a new suit, but the aunt instructed, “Only make the outer robe. Inside, whether leather, cotton, or padded, simply cover the new suit with the outer robe—no matter what old garments underneath.” Some joked of Ruiyun: “Gold on the outside, rotten cotton within.” Yet the cotton was not rotten—like a circle of scholars with archaic minds who adopted a veneer of modern learning and scorn all else. That suit became her staple for autumn, winter, and spring—people teased, “That’s a three‑season suit, just not for summer.” Ruiyun wept many times; at home in a patched old coat, she leaped about delighted. Never before had she cried for lack of clothes, yet now, because her garments were slightly unfashionable, she wept bitterly. This shows times and environments change, life must adapt. Ruiyun, a naturally carefree girl, one moment cried, the next laughed and played.

Financial Market: Interest Rates Turning Around, Liquidity as Before 114

Market Overview

Yesterday’s foreign‑currency interest market was brisk, with banks actively buying and money exchanges and peers engaging in mutual supply and demand. The momentum was strong, and the morning session’s conditions were not weaker than the usual two sessions combined. This indicates that market sentiment now carries hopes of a turnaround. Accordingly, interest rates rose with the trend; though not skyrocketing, they climb step by step daily—a positive sign for the market’s prospects. As for liquidity, with ample upfront reserves but persistent mismatches in supply and demand, improvement remains difficult.

Interest‑Rate Swaps

In the morning, due to very active foreign‑exchange transactions, swap rates rose to 0.719625%. Compared with the previous day’s increase of 0.0625%, they have risen by over one percentage point between the two markets. Interbank silver and account transfers remain conducted on white‑letter credit; no “water” charges or bank quotations were in evidence.

Sub‑Coin Market

Jiangnan small silver coins rose by 1 point from the previous day to 6.275%, with trading volume of 3,500 taels. Guangdong small silver coins also gained 1 point to 5.97%, with 85,000 taels traded. At midday, trading had not yet begun, so no market price was available. In the copper coin bulk market, prices rose by 500 cash to 402,500 cash per string, with trading exceeding 31,000 strings.

Early‑market draft receipts totaled 1,065,000 taels, with notes at 5,000 taels—net down by 625. Off‑market notes amounted to 85,000 taels. Banks’ foreign‑currency trading saw purchases of 700,000 taels and sales of 650,000 taels. Banks’ foreign accounts settlements exceeded 1.2 million taels. Gold Price Exceeds Ninety Taels 115

Efforts to cover short positions and reverse long positions converged, and on the 20th the standard gold price rose with increasing fervor. Market sentiment reached optimism, and prices moved beyond the 490-tael mark for foreign transactions—likely because buying outpaced selling. Exchange drafts also tightened. Traders scrambled to settle accounts; confidence remained high. Firms such as Hengxing, Yuda, Yuanmaoyong, Fuxingyong, Jin’an, and others had already purchased, while some covered shorts and reversed long positions. Sellers including Fuchang, Yuanheng, Zhifengyong, and others liquidated holdings. Prices fluctuated unpredictably, with daily swings totaling over four taels. In the early session, large-unit quotes narrowed, immediately rising by over two taels, but then oscillated within two taels. By midday the market had reached a peak, briefly surpassing ninety-two taels. Exchange bills strengthened further; the current London gold settlement stood at only one shilling eleven and five‑eighth pence, and Japanese gold required 102.5 fen per tael. Later, the price leaped to ninety-three taels as buyers grew active. When exchange liquidity eased, it fell two taels, then recovered by more than one tael to ninety‑one taels and sixty‑seven cents. London silver bars recently contracted by 1.25 for spot and 1.875 for forward, while silver prices dropped three‑eighths and exchange rates eased slightly.

Open trades leapt, with the April gold contract opening stronger by two taels at 490 taels and 4 cents. Unsettled positions fell from 90 taels and 9 cents to 88 taels and 6 cents, but bids from Guangdong Bank and Yuda, among others, supported prices. Sumitomo covered Japanese gold at 101.9375 per tael, so when the shilling was declared it equated to 89 taels and 67 cents. Despite Japanese gold ticking down by 1.25, it held firm, later rising to 91 taels and 4 cents. Meanwhile, Hua & McCallum offered June sterling at 11.8125 pence; HSBC also offered May at the same rate. With small accounts selling, prices dropped over two taels to 88 taels and 8 cents by 10:45. In the afternoon, April sterling traded around 11.75 pence; June by China & Eastern at 11.75; and Hua Shang Bank sold Japanese gold at 102.225 per tael. After 11, the market tightened step by step, buyers remained active, and the price jumped over three taels to 92 taels and 4 cents at midday.

At close the spot gold price rose by three taels and three cents. The afternoon opening was at 92 taels, briefly dipping to 92 taels and 2 cents, but with strong buying by Jin’an, Hengxing, and others, and a steady exchange market, Mitsui bought Japanese gold at 102.375, and HSBC offered US dollars for April at 48 yuan. All banks sold sterling around 11.6875 pence. By 2:30 the price stood at 93 taels, but Yuanheng and others favored selling, and market sentiment soured, pushing it down over two taels. It fluctuated within one tael and a few cents, then by around 4 o’clock dipped to 90 taels and 6 cents. Minor accounts bought, and though foreign exchange remained balanced, gold was pulled up by over a tael to 92 taels, closing at 4:20 at 91 taels and 7 cents—an increase of three taels and three cents over the 19th.

Ornamental gold reached seventy yuan per tael. In one day the highest price was 93 taels and the lowest 88 taels and 6 cents, a swing of 4 taels and 4 cents. The quoted rate was revised to 498 taels and 6 cents, given Japanese gold marking down by 1.25. Early market withheld by nine taels; by midday it only withheld by six taels. Ledger quotes ranged from 92 taels to 91 taels; replenishment at 92 taels. Afternoon replenishment was also at 92 taels. Quoted suppression at 67 taels. Commodity gold opened at 90 taels and 4 cents, fell 2 taels and 6 cents by midday, 2 taels and 1 cent in the afternoon, and closed down 1 tael and 7 cents. Jewelry shops raised ornamental gold by half a yuan per tael to seventy yuan.

Dalian Bank sold three thousand bars of various grades; Hengxing, Shengda, and Yuda bought over five thousand bars. Yuanmaoyong, Fuxingyong, Tianxiang, and others also purchased. Fuchang, Yuanheng, Zhifengyong, Wanxing, Shunli, Taixing, and others all sold. Yuantai and Yichang saw some mismatches in spot trades. Dalian Bank offloaded eighteen hundred bars and bought four to five hundred. In the morning Hengxing sold one thousand bars. In the afternoon inter-dealer trades saw Hengxing, Yuda, Jindayong, Jin’an, etc., buying; Yuanheng, Yuanmaoyong, Shunli, Wanxing, Yuantai, Zhifengyong, Yuanchengyong, etc., selling. Dalian Bank still sold twelve hundred bars and bought over three hundred bars.

Yarn and Cotton Yarn Markets Suffering Intense Declines 120

The yarn market is deeply weakened with severe declines, due to weak overseas demand and poor news. Buyers remain cautious, actual sales are light, and abundant stock creates panic. The outlook is pessimistic as public sentiment favors sellers, renewing calls for fifty taels. Looking back on last year’s situation, one cannot help but reflect on dramatic change.

Spot combed yarn plunged over two taels. At the Exchange, Morning Standard Yarn opened with American cotton weakening, down three to four cents. Soon “No. 3” yarn fell sharply, with thin sales and abundant seller supply. New sellers entered; the market suddenly turned, falling 1 tael 5 mace 6 cents. In the afternoon it remained very soft, continuing losses of seven cents to 1 tael 3 cents per month, with distant months dropping past four taels. Total turnover was 23,950 bales. Closing prices of standard yarn:

• May: 1532, down 26; volume 24,000

• June: 1533, down 23; volume 16,700

• March: 1539, down 29; volume 41,250

• April: 1535, down 24; volume 37,000

• February: 1558, down 20; volume 23,100

• July: 1534, down 22; volume 10,800

Spot sales were even thinner, as futures collapsed, prompting suspensions and slight purchases by mills. Total day’s spot turnover was 885 bales, with declines of five cents to over one tael. Among customer districts: 220 bales from the interior, 40 from Changsha, 62 from Qingdao, 10 from Ningbo, 45 from Xuzhou, 30 from Wuhu, 50 from Jiangxi, 35 from Sichuan, 10 from Qinhuangdao, 18 from Fujian, 15 from Niuzhuang, 15 from Xiamen, 25 from Nanyang, 25 from Shantou; traders took 275 bales.

Cotton Market Under Heavy Pressure 121

American cotton continues downward; the yarn market’s tragedy dampens sentiment. Spot trading stalls; market confidence weakens. Holders remain steady, so the cotton market is less drastic than yarn. Demand is passive; occasional Indian cotton deals offer little morale. The market remains stuck.

Standard cotton followed overseas declines. At the Wuhan Exchange, morning U.S. cotton falls and weak yarn markets softened sentiment; losses of three to four cents across months. In the afternoon, buyers held firm, preventing proportional declines; each month recovered five to ten cents. Total turnover was 41,700 piculs. Closing standard cotton prices:

• July: 342.0, down 40; volume 30,200

Spot cotton trading was muted. On the pier, Xingxing delivered 500 cases at 34 taels 5 cents through the Shenxin mill; goods consigned to the factory.

Grain Flour Prices Continue to Decline with Foreign Wheat 123

Slight recovery before close.

In yesterday’s flour market, after the morning opening, there was a sudden sharp fall in line with foreign wheat quotes which retreated by one yuan. Speculators believed foreign wheat was tipping, reversing the old pattern of larger falls and smaller rises, making foreign wheat arrivals to China seem possible. This shook confidence and led to sharp declines at opening, which for domestic conditions was welcome news for the poor. In the afternoon, however, after floating positions covered shorts and some mills bought, the market stabilized slightly and began to recover. Ultimately, compared with the previous day’s close, this month’s contract was down only 1.5 cents; March/April down 1.5 cents; May down 1.25 cents; June down 5 cents; with July unchanged.

Wheat Market Hard to Rally Further 124

Foreign wheat frequently down slightly; spot arrivals flood; futures collapse.

Yesterday spot wheat plunged uniformly after foreign wheat quotes fell by one yuan, triggering a wave of liquidations. Recent attempts to rally by external interests failed as the environment turned adverse. Domestic wheat prices, now high, encouraged holders to offer for sale, leading to heavy arrivals from various sources—the primary cause of the wheat market’s weakness. The month’s contract bottomed at 5 taels 1.7 cents, recovered 1 cent at close but was still down 4 cents from the previous day. March down 6 cents; April down 5 cents; June and July roughly even. The spot market is saturated by stocks; mills lack appetite, thus prices must fall further to resume activity.

Rice Market: Northern Steady, Southern Firm 125

In Zhabei yesterday, various shipments arrived, totaling around thirty vessels; retail sales lacked the previous day’s vigor. Northern high‑grade japonica remained firm; second‑grade, after consecutive rises, saw buyers cautious, anticipating corrections. But sellers, mindful of costs, refused to lower, so transactions decreased. Standard indica needs were light; though supplies were scant, prices lagged slightly. Yangdu indica remained firm; sellers attempted to corner, keeping the market steady. Local mixed indica sales proceeded; river shipments were few, prices similar to the last close. Southern Shanghai imports were scarce, but with northern demand firm, they rose neatly.

Foreign Rice Futures Mixed 126

In yesterday’s foreign rice market, only small Cochin broken rice—3,000 bags—was quoted; other grades were unavailable due to scarcity. Futures trading was good, but small broken rice spot had two months of supply and heavy selling, so futures softened and spot was weak. Only Saigon rice attracted inquiries, but with scarce supplies, firms refused to sell, pushing futures up another four to five cents. Thai broken rice also sold off, depressing the market. Totals: spot small broken 3,000 bags traded at 13.60–13.20 yuan. Silk and Tea: Anhui Tea New Crop News 127

Frost damage from snow likely to sharply reduce output.

(A Feb 14 dispatch from Wuyuan) Anhui’s key tea counties—Wuyuan, Qimen, Xiuning, Shexian, and Qiupu—produce famed Qimen black and Wuyuan green teas, prized by British, American, and French merchants. Annual output exceeds 400,000 chests, 40% of China’s total. Shanghai market swings depend on Anhui tea performance, so any shortfall draws attention. This winter’s extreme cold and heavy snow have long‑pressed tea trees, causing frost damage: red leaves falling, and with lingering cold into spring, an estimated one‑fifth of the tea trees have wilted—unprecedented in thirty years. Locals estimate spring growth will be far weaker and yields significantly lower. Even with intensive fertilization and care, output might not exceed 70% of normal.

Huang and Bai Factories Distribute Smoothly, Prices Firm 128

In yesterday’s local foreign silk market, conditions were firm and smooth. European firms, seeing overseas raw silk sales rising sharply and stocks low, grew optimistic. Silk supplies are limited, bolstering sentiment. Yesterday, Hua Tong and others bought thirty bales of Shanghai White Factory silk; Xinfu and others bought twenty bales of Sichuan Huang Factory silk; Xiangli and others bought sixteen bales of cotton‑reeled silk, all at firm prices.
