Local News

Belgian Minister Arrived in Shanghai Yesterday 129

The new Norwegian minister will come to Shanghai today.

The Belgian Minister to China, Mr. Waldeck, and his wife, traveling from Peking to Qingdao on the Fengtian Maru, arrived in Shanghai yesterday afternoon.

Norway’s new minister to China, Mr. Abell, arrived aboard the Australasia Queen mail steamer today to assume his post. He is scheduled to enter the capital on the 23rd and present his credentials on the morning of the 24th.

Wang Zhengting Arrived in Shanghai This Morning 130

Hosted a Banquet for Mr. and Mrs. Li Jinlun that Evening

Foreign Minister Wang Zhengting had originally planned to arrive in Shanghai yesterday to attend the Red Cross Society’s welcome assembly for Vice President Yu Qiaqing’s assumption of duties. Due to pressing matters preventing him from freeing himself, he instead came last night by night train with Sun Ke, Song Ziwen, and Wei Daoming, arriving at the port at seven this morning. At eight this evening, he will entertain the newly appointed Vice Minister of Foreign Affairs, Mr. Li Jinlun, and his wife at his private residence on Guba Road. Because Mr. Wang must return to Nanjing on the evening of the 23rd in order to present his credentials to the Norwegian envoy in Beijing on the 24th, he will depart promptly that night.

River and Sea Shipping News 131

The day before yesterday, a local steamship company here received a report from Nanjing stating that a Yangtze River steamer, having passed Huangshi Harbor eastbound below Hankou, was suddenly fired upon from both riverbanks. Fortunately the vessel had increased speed and no crew were injured, and since there were no passengers aboard at the time, there was no loss. Incidents of sporadic rifle fire along the route have resumed in recent days. To ensure safety, operators of all river steamers have agreed to install steel plates outside the engine rooms on vessels traveling upriver, so as to avoid danger.

Railway materials for the Longhai Railway have all been purchased abroad and unloaded here in Shanghai. Last winter, the royal shipping company of that country arranged eight commercial steamers specifically to transport rolling stock and steel rails for the Longhai line; three of these have already arrived, each fully laden with Longhai Railway cargo. As usual, they unloaded at Pukou before forwarding to Zhengzhou and other destinations. This latest shipment includes four locomotives and over two thousand tons of railway materials, which have just arrived. After discussion yesterday, it was decided to unload these at Wusong and await the opening of the Longhai line before transferring them onward.

Outbound cargo for Haizhou has sharply declined. Most of the goods shipped from Shanghai to Haizhou are transshipments bound for Kaifeng, Luoyang, Shaanxi, and Gansu. Three steamers now serve that route, but this month each has made only a single voyage, and outbound cargo has been scant. Recently, due to the unsettled situation, all Haizhou transshipment cargo destined for Kaifeng, Luoyang, Shaanxi, and Gansu has been halted. Whether the Haizhou steamers can resume voyages after returning to Shanghai this time remains uncertain.

Suspension and Resumption of Chinese Steamers. The China Merchants Steam Navigation Company’s Guangli steamer, returning from Guangdong to Shanghai via Amoy, was reported yesterday to have been requisitioned at Xiamen for troop transport to Quanzhou, making it difficult to sail to Shanghai for the time being. As for the trade steamers Gongping and Guangda, which had been scheduled to depart for Beijing from Shanghai upon arrival, the Navigation Department yesterday notified that they need not make the voyage to the capital. Consequently, the Merchants Bureau plans to reassign those two vessels to the South and North China coastal lines to meet passenger and cargo transport needs.

More than Eighty Commercial Associations Call for Peace 132

Over eighty groups—including the Southern Goods Subcommittee, Dental Articles Subcommittee, Silk and Satin Subcommittee, Poultry and Vegetable Stall Subcommittee, Fresh Sausage Subcommittee, Duck Vendor Subcommittee, and others under the Shanghai Special Municipality Chamber of Commerce—sent telegrams yesterday to President Chiang Kai-shek and Deputy Commander Yan Xishan of the National Government, appealing for peace.

Municipal Executive Committee Sends Another Telegram of Earnest Advice to Yan Xishan 133

The Shanghai Special Municipality Executive Committee previously sent a telegram on the 15th to Yan Xishan requesting clarification of the true situation, but received no reply. Yesterday (the 20th), they sent another earnest advisory telegram stating: “The nation is in crisis and summons you to return immediately. Comrade Yan Baichuan in Taiyuan, please take note: from the moment your telegram was sent, the entire country was alarmed, interpreting your call for courtesy and yielding as a breach of discipline. Should you stray from the path and not return, you will surely precipitate renewed internal strife. Since we had not yet seen the original message, we hesitated to believe it. We respectfully inquire again and trust you will examine this carefully. Now the newspapers report extensive exchanges of telegrams, your statements grow ever more extraordinary, and your positions ever more erroneous. Our committee, holding fast to the Supreme Leader’s teachings of loyalty and sincerity, earnestly hopes you will not, for political expediency, follow in the footsteps of Feng Tang or Zhang Gui, thereby undermining your own revolutionary record. We therefore dare to summarize the essential principles and offer this renewed counsel:

“A revolutionary party member must embody the character of revolution, and a revolutionary soldier must uphold revolutionary morality. The revolutionary cause exists to benefit others, not oneself. Thus, the success of the revolution depends entirely on whether the nation and the people have been saved, without regard to personal considerations. By this standard, the so‑called ‘revolutionary character and morality’ means: 1) Serving the people without seeking shortcuts or personal comfort—not about courtesy, yielding, or resting—but about sacrificing for the nation, unafraid of hardship and unyielding, not compromise or hedging. 2) Uniting and obeying under the party’s principles, not opportunism, deception, or disciplinary breaches—definitely not intrigue or clandestine maneuvering. Our party, following the Supreme Leader’s final instructions, has struggled steadfastly for years, never yielding. There is now a fragile opportunity for peaceful unification that could serve as the basis for our tutelage and reconstruction. Comrade Chiang Kai-shek, entrusted by the party and the nation with heavy responsibilities, works ceaselessly; how could he tolerate being overthrown? Your pretext of courtesy and yielding as a condition for resignation is mere opportunism and a refusal to revolutionize. You even propose entrapping Comrade Chiang to deprive the revolution of its leader—this is a grave error. The party has a history and an organizational system: three terms of Central Committee, unanimously elected at the Third National Congress with the trust of all party comrades, exercising authority without reproach. If, as you propose, the three-term plenary, senior leaders, or even founding elders each voice conflicting dreams, the system will collapse, regulations be rendered meaningless, and our governance perish. Cooperation or hedging may be tolerable; calling it loyalty to the revolution is not. These are two serious mistakes. In a successful revolution, party members have no personal liberty: the Supreme Leader’s final teachings remain clear. Comrades, could you really forget that our warlords have only just been swept away and rebellion nearly extinguished? At this critical juncture, if one seeks to save the nation by abandoning party adherence and the rule of law, there is no alternative path. To speak of ‘respecting the party’ requires defending the Central Authority; to speak of ‘upholding the law’ requires obedience to orders. Your Taiyuan pledge of allegiance and Zhengzhou support of the central army remain fresh, yet you now call the suppression of rebellion a ‘civil war’ intended to stop disorder and threaten the Central Authority, arrogantly opposing the National Government, wielding troops in territorial fiefdoms. Such defiance shows contempt for central orders and free action—where is your unity? If your heart is not set on opportunism or deception, you would not commit illegal or disorderly acts. These are three errors. Our committee deeply remembers your past contributions and cannot bear to see you destroy your own standing, bringing pain to friends and joy to foes. We propose three courses:

“First, if you repent, you should clearly seek national rest by first preserving unity—upholding the central authority. You might emulate the great statesmen of Japan who relinquished military power, entered Tokyo to serve, supported the central government, and facilitated tutelage. Second, if you still wish to remain in the North, you must lead your troops in obedience, diligently pursue governance, and stabilize civilian life. Third, if not, you must immediately resign and return state affairs to the Central Committee, fulfilling your promise of yielding, even if it means sacrificing personal liberty, yet remaining a man of integrity.

“Should you fail to do so and persist in erroneous arguments and frightening rhetoric, confusing right and wrong, betraying discipline, pressuring responsible central officials, and shielding those who defy orders, you will inevitably tread the path of counter‑revolution and become the author of renewed internal chaos—a fate our comrades cannot bear. You must choose your course wisely.”

Issued under the seal of the Shanghai Special Municipality Executive Committee of the Chinese Nationalist Party.

Red Cross Society Held Meeting Yesterday to Welcome Yu Qiaqing’s Assumption of Duties 134

Accounts of the Relief Fund Department Thoroughly Audited

Bylaw Revisions for the Red Cross Society Decided

The Chinese Red Cross Society held a welcome assembly at Yipinxiang yesterday at three o’clock in the afternoon to honor Vice President Yu Qiaqing’s assumption of duties. Dozens of attendees included Wang Yiting, Wang Xiaolai, Chen Shiguang, and representatives from various branch societies. President Yan Huiqing also sent a representative. Vice President Wang Zhengting was unable to attend due to pressing governmental affairs and specially telegrammed Chen Shiguang to represent him. Chairman of the General Assembly Wang Yiting delivered the opening address, after which Yu Qiaqing stood to speak. He said briefly: “I am truly unworthy of your warm welcome. Until now, the Vice Presidents in Shanghai have been responsible for the Society’s work. As the Red Cross has an international character and carries significant weight in national and diplomatic matters, and because the Society holds over one hundred thousand yuan in cash and assets worth hundreds of thousands more, the future prospects for our work are bright. My previous sudden resignation was due to my objection to the Relief Fund Department being established within the Red Cross Society. While I do not oppose relief work, embedding the Relief Fund Department in the Red Cross has led to all annual Society revenues being diverted to relief expenditures, causing a surge in spending and depletion of income sources. Our hundreds of thousands in funds could be exhausted in a few years, forcing the Red Cross to effectively collapse. Since the Relief Fund Department was approved by the Standing Council, I felt compelled by the gravity of the situation and the danger ahead to resign and to halt further allocations to relief, to prevent the Society’s foundations from being shaken. The Standing Council then formed a provisional executive committee to reorganize and revise the bylaws. The Departments of Diplomacy, Internal Affairs, and Health also dispatched personnel to Shanghai to investigate the Society’s operations, concluding that the bylaws must be amended as soon as possible. I fully support the establishment of an executive committee, for the Red Cross is an international enterprise that cannot be monopolized by a few individuals. Without bylaw revisions, there can be no solution, so I could only resign.”

Discussion then turned to proposals from the branches to request that the President conduct a thorough audit of the Relief Fund Department’s accounts, publishing detailed expenditures. The Chairman will report on this. The Relief Fund Department is now concluding its work; in future the Economic Board will examine the accounts in detail, followed by an auditor’s verification. The meeting adjourned at six. It was learned that on April 20 in Shanghai the Third General Assembly of all members will convene to discuss the bylaw revisions and elect officers. Yu Qiaqing’s vice‑presidential role will last only until that assembly.

Another Breakdown in Sino-Japanese Treaty Revision 136

In the ongoing Sino-Japanese treaty revision, the two countries’ diplomatic authorities have gone back and forth several times, and tariff issues have been negotiated to agreement. It was planned that other matters would be discussed in detail this week when the Japanese chargé d’affaires Kōkō—a shorthand for Reijun Shigemitsu—arrived in Beijing to continue consultations. However, in recent days the chargé has repeatedly postponed his departure and made no firm preparations to travel to the capital. While Japan is undergoing elections and its political situation is unsettled, the Foreign Ministry has used China’s internal turbulence as a pretext for delay, which is the main reason for the postponements. The Japanese, having no genuine intent to relinquish navigation, judicial, or tariff rights, must resist treaty changes, hence their continual stalling. From recent Japanese attitudes, a breakdown has indeed occurred and negotiations seem likely to stall indefinitely.

Two Silk Industry Groups Pay Attention to Sino-Japanese Treaty Revision 137

The Silk and Satin Subcommittee of the Shanghai Special Municipality Chamber of Commerce and the China Domestic Silk Shanghai Relief Association jointly telegrammed the Ministry of Foreign Affairs yesterday, requesting that in the upcoming Sino-Japanese treaty the silk tariff rates between the two countries be set on the principle of equal reciprocity. Their telegram reads: “To Minister Wang of the MFA: We understand that Korea is a major consumer of silk, and Chinese silk exports there once totaled two million yuan annually—of great importance to our national economy and people’s livelihood. Since 1923, Japan’s protectionist policy has restricted Chinese silk, imposing punitive duties as high as 100%. Over the past six or seven years all sales to Korea have ceased, causing unbearable hardship for our industry and unemployment. Despite repeated appeals by our silk associations and protests to the diplomatic authorities, nothing has changed. Now that a new treaty is imminent, although the Japanese have acknowledged our tariff autonomy, they demand a Most‑Favored‑Nation rate on dozens of varieties. They continue to exact punitive duties and unjustly coerce us. How can this be called equal reciprocity? We therefore petition your honorable ministry: when the new treaty is formally signed this autumn, silk tariff rates between our two countries must adhere to equal reciprocity—if they do not lower theirs, we will not lower ours. We will vigorously defend our position without concession, to protect our silk industry and uphold national dignity. We await your kind attention.”

Steel Rolling Mill Temporarily Suspends Operations 138

Awaiting the Arrival of New Plant Manager Zhou for Further Decisions

After the resignation of Plant Manager Hu Shuhua, his successor has not yet assumed office, and the mill’s funds have been exhausted. On the 17th, Hu went to Beijing to seek guidance from the Ministry of Military Affairs. He returned to Shanghai yesterday morning and, finding no solution to the funding problem and with the unsettled situation making financing especially difficult, decided to suspend operations temporarily. Yesterday afternoon, upon arriving at the Gaochang Temple mill site, he issued a notice stating: “Due to blocked product sales and financial difficulties, we applied for emergency funds on the 13th, and on the 14th received a telegram from the Arsenal Administration that no funds were available. If operations cannot be sustained, temporary suspension is authorized.” After Hu personally went to Beijing for confirmation, the Department head verbally approved the suspension, pending the arrival of new Plant Manager Zhou to assess conditions and determine further measures. Accordingly, operations will be suspended from the 21st of this month until Manager Zhou arrives to decide on next steps. This is hereby announced.

Ministry of Agriculture and Mining Approves Interim Measures for Inspection of Plant Diseases and Pests 147

(Interim Measures for the Inspection of Plant Diseases and Pests by the Agricultural Produce Inspection Office of the Ministry of Agriculture and Mining) Approved by the Ministry of Agriculture and Mining on February 15, Year 19 of the Republic, to be promulgated and implemented by the Ministry.

(Article 1) The Agricultural Produce Inspection Office of the Ministry of Agriculture and Mining, pursuant to the Agricultural Produce Inspection Regulations of the Ministry, shall conduct inspections for plant diseases and pests on agricultural products.

(Article 2) “Plant diseases and pests” as referred to in these measures denote harmful pathogens and insects to agricultural products; other organisms deemed harmful may also be inspected under these measures.

(Article 3) The agricultural products subject to inspection under these measures are provisionally designated as: livestock meat, hides and skins, aquatic products, silkworm eggs, bees, food grains, fruits, flowers and plants, seedlings, seeds, medicinal plants, timber, cotton products, and tobacco leaves. The sequence of inspections for these categories shall be subject to Ministry approval upon proposal by the Inspection Office.

(Article 4) Anyone transporting the products listed in the preceding article must complete the inspection application form prescribed by the Inspection Office, and present it along with the shipping manifest, inspection fee, and license fee at the designated inspection office. Foreign inspection certificates, if carried, must also be submitted. Officials may examine on site or collect samples for laboratory testing.

(Article 5) [Omitted in original; numbering jumps from Article 4 to Article 6.]

(Article 6) After inspection, if products are found to harbor harmful pathogens or insects, they may be ordered to be sent to a specified location for fumigation, disinfection, or destruction, or banned from transport. Costs of transport for fumigation, disinfection, destruction, or return transit shall be borne by the applicant; holding fees for detained livestock shall likewise be the applicant’s responsibility.

(Article 7) If the Inspection Office deems it necessary to prevent the spread of dangerous diseases or pests, it may, with Ministry approval, issue an order prohibiting the import of such products from affected regions.

(Article 8) Specimens of pathogens or pests imported for academic research must first obtain a permit from the Ministry of Agriculture and Mining, and upon arrival must be reported to the Inspection Office and found error‑free before entry is permitted.

(Article 9) Even if products pass inspection, they must be re‑inspected if re‑packaged or combined; re‑inspection fees shall be waived.

(Article 10) For inspections, the Inspection Office shall temporarily reduce the fee prescribed by Article 4 of the Agricultural Produce Inspection Regulations from 2% to 1%. The license fee shall be one national currency yuan per certificate in accordance with Article 6 of the Regulations.

(Article 11) Inspection fees are non‑refundable whether the products pass or fail inspection; however, if the license is refused, the license fee may be refunded.

(Article 12) The Inspection Office shall conduct spot checks and random inspections in various locations at any time.

(Article 13) When necessary, the Inspection Office may entrust local government entomology bureaus or other agencies and companies to carry out inspection, fumigation, disinfection, and related procedures, subject to prior Ministry approval of the entrustment method.

(Article 14) Anyone transporting products subject to inspection who violates these measures or commits concealment, fraud, or other misconduct shall be dealt with in accordance with the Inspection of Agricultural Produce Penalties Regulations.

(Article 15) If an inspector intentionally obstructs or extorts a merchant, the merchant may lodge a complaint with the Inspection Office, which shall forward it to the court for punishment.

(Article 16) Agricultural products that pass inspection shall be listed and reported publicly every half‑month, and at the end of each month a summary shall be submitted to the Ministry for record.

(Article 17) For fumigation, disinfection, and related procedures, the Inspection Office may, according to the nature of the items, separately formulate operational rules.

(Article 18) Matters not covered by these measures may be amended at any time upon Ministry submission.

(Article 19) These measures shall take effect on the date of Ministry approval.

Municipal Government Orders Civil Servants to Use Domestic Products 149

Our nation’s industries lag behind, and foreign goods flood the markets. In clothing and accessories, imported items compete to showcase novelty, and our citizens eagerly purchase them. According to customs reports, in the seventeenth fiscal year imports of foreign goods totaled 1,195,969,000 taels. Among these, silk, hemp, cotton, wool, and leather products alone amounted to more than 360,957,400 taels, demonstrating that spending on clothing and accessories is a major reason for our trade deficit. Recently, to alleviate the financial situation, the Ministry of Commerce petitioned the National Government to issue a clear order requiring all civil and military officials, within and outside the capital, to strictly use domestic products from the date of promulgation in order to stem the outflow of funds. The National Government has issued this directive for nationwide compliance. Upon receipt of this order, the Municipal Government immediately instructed all its civil servants to comply fully.

News from the Swiss Industrial Exhibition in Shanghai 150

Exhibition Period: April 26 to May 6

Location: Still in Basel

Swiss industry, whether producing large machinery or small watches, pursues excellence in every aspect. Its industrial progress is remarkable. Exports include power-driven machines, electrical appliances, instruments, clocks and watches, gemstones, chemical and pharmaceutical products, silk and woolen textiles, embroidered goods, boots and shoes, chocolate, condensed milk, and butter. All these items share superior material and craftsmanship. Since Switzerland must import its raw materials, its global competitiveness relies entirely on the refinement of its manufactured goods.

Since the fifteenth century, Basel has held an annual industrial exhibition. The upcoming 14th exhibition runs from April 26 to May 6 this year, again at its traditional venue. Reorganized in 1917, the exhibition’s reputation has grown. Basel, on the Rhine River, is a hub of railways and shipping lines, and a center of finance and industry, making it an ideal location. The exhibition is noted for its complete facilities and exquisite displays, praised by the international business community. Visitors can, within a few hours, view the full range of Swiss products.

All exhibits are Swiss-made and presented by dozens of firms, serving as a forum for exchanging industrial and commercial knowledge and fostering progress. Originally intended for domestic participants, the exhibition’s strategic location has made it an international trade center, attracting over 300,000 visitors. As a purely commercial event, its aim is to expand the market for Switzerland’s refined yet affordable goods and cultivate international trade relations.

From 1923 to 1927, ten trillion Swiss francs were allocated to build a dedicated exhibition hall and equipment. At the exhibition, staff extend every convenience to foreign visitors seeking to purchase goods or tour the exhibits. A special office handles facility rentals; typists and interpreters are available; telephone and telegraph services are especially convenient. A permanent inquiry desk answers questions about Swiss products at any time, without charge. Attendees may also tour Switzerland’s famous mountains, rivers, and scenic sites.

The Swiss Consulate General in Shanghai, at No. 1415 Xiamei Road, is pleased to provide information at any time regarding exhibition details, lodging, and transport.

Borrowing Funds to Build Housing for Commoners 152

Submitted to the Construction Discussion Council by Mayoral Order

In order to build housing for commoners, the Committee for Planning Commoners’ Housing previously deliberated on borrowing methods, construction, and repayment with interest, and submitted a proposal to the Mayor for review. We report that throughout the city—east, west, north, and south—grass huts stand densely side by side, visible everywhere, totaling approximately 22,000 households. Most residents are laborers and porters. These huts are extremely fire-prone, and any disaster can easily burn hundreds of homes, causing unimaginable suffering. Additionally, the low living standards and poor sanitation among residents, especially in summer and autumn, breed epidemics. Unemployed vagrants also congregate, and with no household registration or street numbers, police patrols are difficult. This is a serious public security issue and tarnishes the city’s image at a critical stage of municipal development.

In advanced industrial countries, factory owners often provide adequate housing for workers. Our industry is nascent and factory capital insufficient to care for commoners’ livelihoods. At this initial stage of municipal construction, the government must urgently build housing for commoners and eliminate the grass huts. However, the scale of this undertaking is immense. To build over one hundred housing units and a large assembly hall will require more than 20,000 yuan. Given the current municipal treasury’s deficit, we cannot address this urgent need without securing substantial additional funds. Therefore, we propose two methods: borrowing from the financial sector and charitable organizations, and requesting the treasury to allocate annual appropriations.

We plan to borrow 500,000 yuan from various sectors and request the treasury to provide 100,000 yuan, to build 3,000 housing units. The borrowed funds will bear an annual interest rate to be paid from rental income, with the principal to be repaid by the treasury over five or ten years, at 100,000 yuan or 50,000 yuan per year. For these reasons, it is appropriate to draft a schedule for borrowing, repayment, and interest, and submit it for approval. If approved, a joint proposal will then be presented to the Construction Discussion Council for decision, so that commoners’ housing can be implemented in stages. Whether this is advisable, we respectfully request your judgment.

After review, the Mayor found the plan sound in principle but noted the treasury’s deficit and the difficulty of borrowing—especially at a time of bond issuance—and instructed that proposals related to the livelihood of commoners be submitted to the Construction Discussion Council for deliberation.

Wang Leping and Others Were Encoffined Yesterday 153

Wang Leping, a former member of the second Central Executive Committee, together with his friend Pan Xingjian and their servant Kang Ruizhai, were assassinated at 11:30 p.m. on the 18th while at the salon located at No. 314, Xiamen Lane, on Ma Road in the French Concession. The encoffining of the servant Kang and the man found roadside is expected to occur today.

Designated Loading and Unloading Areas for Wusong Bamboo and Timber Rafts 155

Regulations by the Port Authority

The Shanghai Special Municipality Port Authority has regulated the northern bank of the Wusong (Suzhou) River, from the vicinity of Xinjialaji Bridge on Guangfu Road westward to the Guangzhao Villa area. The thoroughfare is busy, the river channel narrow, and vessel traffic congested. Along the riverbank, bamboo and timber dealers have erected rafts and arbitrarily scattered them across the water, obstructing navigation and causing silt accumulation. Immediate enforcement is necessary to maintain traffic.

Based on the Regulations for the Control of Bamboo and Timber Rafts and practical conditions, the authority has formulated these measures: each dealer’s loading and unloading area may not exceed 100 Shanghai feet in length and 20 Shanghai feet in width. For each delivery, the Port Authority will provide a report form for dealers to complete and submit. Officers will inspect and set specific loading and unloading dates. All rafts must be completely removed by those dates. Outside the permitted dates, no materials may be piled on the riverbank. Violators will be fined. These measures have been approved by the Municipal Government and publicly announced. On the 21st of this month, officers will inspect the riverbank areas along the Wusong River to enforce the designated zones. After this cleanup, navigation will be greatly facilitated.

Municipal Social Affairs Bureau Processes Accountant Registrations 156

The Accountant Regulations were enacted and promulgated some time ago. The Municipal Social Affairs Bureau has received instructions from the Municipal Government to enforce these regulations. Article 2 stipulates that accountants operate under the supervision of the Ministry of Commerce, but provincial or non‑provincial municipal commerce authorities, within the scope of the Ministry’s directives, may also exercise supervisory powers. Article 8 requires that, before beginning their duties, accountants must submit an application letter and credentials to the local commerce authority for verification, after which they are to be entered in the Register of Accountants. Article 10 mandates that provincial or non‑provincial municipal commerce authorities report all accountant registrations and cancellations to the Ministry of Commerce and notify all courts in their jurisdiction. Accordingly, the bureau has processed registrations in compliance with the regulations. To date, many practitioners have registered with the bureau, which has forwarded the records to the Ministry of Commerce and notified the courts, approving them to practice in the city.

It was resolved to ask the Civil Affairs Committee for interpretation.

(6) Zhang Yaoming’s proposal: With epidemic cerebrospinal meningitis rampant in the city, preventive health inoculations should be carried out immediately for workers. The union should request the Municipal Party Department to instruct the Health Bureau to vaccinate workers today to safeguard the populace. It was resolved to forward this to the Civil Affairs Committee for referral to the Health Bureau.

(7) Lu Jingshi and others proposed drafting motions for the sixth municipal representative assembly:

a. Urge the assembly to expedite the establishment of the Shanghai Special Municipality General Union to benefit the labor movement.

b. Urge the assembly to instruct the municipal government that, for any violations of labor and capital regulations, parties refusing to comply with government mediation or arbitration decisions should receive equal penalties.

c. Urge the assembly to instruct party and government organs that both labor and capital must strictly observe government‑mandated rest days, with severe punishment for willful violations.

d. Urge the assembly to instruct party and government organs to provide effective relief to unemployed workers in the city and to establish permanent relief measures.

e. Urge the assembly to propose to the central government the immediate dismissal of Shanghai Police Commissioner Yuan Liang for contempt of party authority and oppression of the populace, to restore good governance.

f. Urge the assembly to instruct municipal government agencies that all mass organizations in the city must adhere to party principles and be accorded equal treatment.

g. Urge the assembly to instruct all levels of municipal party committees to expand and strengthen women’s organizations.

For submissions to the Third Plenary Session of the Central Committee:

a. Request the central government to amend the Trade Union Law to benefit the labor movement.

b. Request the central government to amend the Factory Law to safeguard workers’ welfare.

c. Request the central party committee to prohibit key personnel from holding simultaneous administrative offices, to ensure dedicated responsibilities.

d. Request the central government to vigorously implement the Premier’s agrarian and industrial policies.

e. Request the central government to reclaim all foreign concessions nationwide to strengthen national sovereignty.

f. Request the central government to abolish onerous taxes to relieve the people’s distress.

g. Request the central government to support Chinese industry in alignment with the Premier’s industrial salvation policies.

h. Request the central party to restore the February 7 commemoration as China’s Labor Day.

All motions were put to a vote and adopted.

Pacific Association Luncheon Today 159

The Pacific Association will host a banquet for the Pomona College Far East Inspection Delegation today at 12:15 p.m. at the Kaisai Hotel. The delegation, organized by students to study Eastern lifestyles and key issues—particularly in China—aims to foster closer social and intellectual contact between students of the Eastern and Western hemispheres and thereby support global exchanges of knowledge and mutual understanding. Having been in Shanghai for several days, the delegation will be entertained at today’s luncheon, which includes a lecture by the eminent American historian Dr. Gibbon on the Pacific coast republics of South America.

Local News II

Sensational News in Shanghai: Kidnapping on the Huangpu River 160

Yangsi Township Municipal Committee Member Chen Tianxi

At dusk, encountered bandits in mid-stream

Passengers were forced into the water

The Shen large ferry’s whereabouts unknown

At about 6 p.m. yesterday, midway between Dongjiadu in the southern part of the city and Zhoujiadu in Pudong on the Huangpu River, a violent hijacking and abduction occurred aboard a ferry. Here are the details:

Chen Tianxi, a municipal political committee member of Yangsi Township in Pudong—styled Zixin and from a well-established family—frequently crossed to Puxi due to his official duties. Last night, while on the ferry, he was noticed by bandits and abducted.

Yesterday afternoon, Chen had driven by automobile from his home to Zhoujiadu. After a brief rest, at about 6 p.m. he boarded the large ferry that shuttles between Zhoujiadu and Dongjiadu. When the ferry reached midstream, six bandits armed with handguns appeared. Two of them kept watch over Chen, while the others forced all passengers into the water. Simultaneously, they threatened the ferry’s pilot at the bow, demanding he steer northward. With no choice, the pilot obeyed and steered the vessel north.

Passengers who had dived into the river swam to shore and reported the incident. Many Pudong residents living along the riverbank are skilled swimmers, so they reached land and rushed to inform the Sanqu District Third Police Post in Tangqiao Town. The post commander immediately notified the District 3 General Office, which in turn telephoned the Municipal Headquarters. The Headquarters alerted all Pudong police stations and the river patrol unit to be on alert for interception. It is reported that many passengers were forced into the water, and there are likely casualties.

Upon receiving the alert, the police in each district dispatched officers—many armed with rifles—to patrol the riverbanks. The Headquarters also wired Minhang and Songjiang checkpoints to join the search. At the time of this report’s publication, the ferry had not been located. It is unclear whether the bandits sailed it through Minhang or Songjiang or disembarked at a secluded riverside location. Our newspaper, upon hearing the news, telephoned each police district to confirm the identity of the abducted official. They confirmed that it was indeed Chen, the municipal committee member of Yangsi Township in Pudong, without doubt.

Case of Steamed-Bone Examination: Corpse Bones Dismembered Inch by Inch 161

Mrs. Chen Sun’s corpse had not fully decomposed

The steaming and bone-examining procedure was quite complex

The removal of skin and flesh was unbearable to behold

The deceased’s left shinbone bore a hooked protrusion.

In the case concerning Mrs. Chen Sun, daughter-in-law of the late “Northern King” Chen Shuangxi—who had operated the Qinglian Pavilion teahouse at Suzhou’s Chang Gate and the Chen Wanxing lime shop at Yadanqiao—and suspected of having died under suspicious circumstances, the Shanghai District Court conducted a steamed-bone examination, details of which were reported yesterday. It has now been ascertained that the alleged incident occurred on the 17th day of the third lunar month last year, while Chen Shuangxi himself fell ill and died on the 18th. On that same day, the Wuxian District Court dispatched personnel to inspect the body and found minor non-fatal injuries on the head, at the corners of the eyes, and on the legs. The cause of death was determined to be suicide by hanging. The Wuxian Court fined the deceased’s stepmother, Mrs. Chen Li, 200 silver dollars for improper custody of the body, and decided not to prosecute in the matter of suicide. However, the deceased’s father, Sun Zhengwen, was dissatisfied and filed an appeal, which the Higher Court promptly dismissed based on the autopsy report. Sun then appealed to the Supreme Court, requesting a steamed-bone examination to uncover the truth. The Supreme Court remanded the case to the Higher Court, which in turn transferred jurisdiction to the Shanghai District Court and ordered it to handle the matter.

Sun Zhengwen named five defendants in his suit. Besides the corpse-porter Chen Xuebǎo, they included the stepmother Mrs. Chen Li and the sister-in-law Mrs. Chen Chang (wife of Chen Ajin). The chief offender was alleged to be the paternal aunt, Mrs. Chen Li, who—though not deeply involved in household affairs—harbored dislike for the deceased, sowed discord, and caused the young couple to live apart for more than three months until the time of death. The aunt was even said to have advocated murder by strangulation. The defendants vehemently denied these charges, insisting that the deceased died at about 4 p.m. entirely by her own hanging and that no one would dare commit murder in broad daylight. Chen Xuebǎo, age twenty-eight, came from Baoying in Jiangbei; the deceased, age twenty-six (twenty-five at the time of death last year), was from Xinghua. She had married into the Chen family on December 18 at age twenty-three and died on the 17th of the third month at twenty-five—just over a year later.

Yesterday at 10 a.m., plaintiffs Sun Zhengwen and his wife, together with defendants Chen Xuebǎo, Mrs. Chen Li, and her son, all reported to the court. They were escorted by Prosecutor Yang Wenjun along with examiners Shen Xiqing, Xuan Yifang, Xuan Zhiming, and others to the forensic examination office. In the presence of both parties, the coffin was opened and the body removed. The outer burial garments were a red silk blanket; beneath were layers of sky-blue satin underskirt, red silk cotton jacket, blue silk padded jacket, white silk-linen shirt; on the lower body were red silk split trousers and white silk-linen underpants, plus an embroidered red skirt; the head wore a lotus-flower hat, and the feet small red shoes. When stripped, the skin and flesh had not decomposed, though the eyes and nose on the face were rotted. Examiners Shen and Xuan then cut away the skin and flesh from the entire body, scraping out the bones and slicing them inch by inch—a sight too gruesome to behold. The complicated procedure continued until after 4 p.m., when they finally declared the examination complete. Each bone was labeled with its name on a bamboo tag and laid out on a mat in the shape of a human form. The removed skin and flesh were placed back into the coffin. Judge Yang ordered that the bones be wrapped in white cloth and also placed into the coffin. The coffin was sealed all around in the presence of both parties, and the ground beside it and the lid were stamped with the lime seal of the Jiangsu–Shanghai District Court to underscore the solemnity. The forensic office’s doors and windows were sealed as well. At 7 a.m. the next day—today—the bones were to be washed and then subjected to a second steam-bone examination in eight steamer baskets. Judicial police were assigned to stand guard in shifts overnight. Judge Yang instructed all parties not to leave. During the initial scraping, it was noted that the deceased had certain bone anomalies: the head of the left tibia bore a hook-like growth, and the third and fourth left ribs were joined in a Y-shaped fork. Examiners Xuan reported that such bone structures had never been seen before.

Chinese Policeman Won’t Yield to Western Constable 162

Western police head offers apology first

Kong Zhaocun receives official commendation

Yesterday the Shanghai Special Municipal Police Bureau issued a directive to all its precincts, stating that according to the report of Captain Tao Guangqu of the Fifth District Station No. 2, on February 8 at about 5 p.m., at the corner of North Sichuan Road and Guangdong Street, station guard Kong Zhaocun reported that a Western constable had stepped forward and barred the sidewalk on the city side at the sluice-gate. Kong asserted that his post lay within Chinese jurisdiction and told the constable he had not crossed any boundary. The constable, however, showed a fierce manner and demanded the guard’s badge number. When Kong replied that he had none, the constable examined him closely and found his number to be 187. Because Kong refused to yield, the constable walked away uttering words about requesting reprimand. The matter was under review when the next morning Captain Odo of the Diswei Road Precinct arrived and stated that the newly appointed Western constable No. 187 had reported that the Chinese guard had overstepped the boundary line, leading to a slight verbal clash when he questioned the guard. The precinct insisted that its border guards were all seasoned veterans who would not stand outside the line. It was found that constable No. 187, being new, was unfamiliar with the boundary and had interfered without cause. He was sternly admonished and ordered not to repeat the offense. The precinct expressed deep regret, offered an apology to the patrol station, and requested that the matter not be reported to higher authorities to avoid friction. The local station commander immediately agreed not to report it. It was noted that the station’s border guards were chosen for their seniority and experience so as to handle such incidents without undermining police authority or national territory. The Western constable was already reprimanded by his precinct commander upon his apology. It was therefore decided that no further action be taken against him. Guard Kong Zhaocun, who firmly asserted sovereign rights without yielding, richly deserved commendation and was to be officially praised. This order was to be communicated to all district commanders.

Murder During New Year’s Eve Feast 163

Sentenced to life imprisonment

Pan Quanyou, a resident of Ningbo, dragged home his wife, surnamed Jiao, because she refused to attend the New Year’s Eve dinner at relatives’ house, stabbed her to death, and was arrested by the Huishan Precinct. His case was transferred to the Temporary Court for inquiry. Yesterday morning, Presiding Officer Ge opened the hearing and pronounced sentence: Pan Quanyou was given life imprisonment, and the murder weapon was confiscated. The bailiffs were ordered to take him to the western jail to await execution of the sentence.

Man Found Hanged on Rooftop Terrace 164

Second landlord suspected

Both plaintiffs could not be summoned

On the rooftop terrace of the house at No. 697 Yunhe Lane, East Yalu Road, a man’s body was found hanged on the 27th of last month. The local precinct identified the deceased as Wu Shunren and sent the body to the forensic office on Felun Road, requesting the Temporary Court to conduct an autopsy and prepare for burial. At that time, the sub-landlord Guan Tiren was summoned for questioning and released for lack of involvement. Recently, two men claiming to be the deceased’s brothers, Wu Dazhi and Wu Jianyin of No. 22 Helin Lane, North Sichuan Road, jointly filed a petition with the Temporary Court, alleging suspicion on the part of the sub-landlord and requesting exhumation and examination. Presiding Officer Gao soon issued summonses for both petitioners to appear at a hearing yesterday morning at 9 a.m., when Guan and the tenants were to testify. Neither Wu Dazhi nor Wu Jianyin appeared. According to a report by Chinese Constable No. 1308 of the Harbin Precinct, summonses were served at Helin Lane but no such lane name existed; the closest address was No. 22 Renzhili, where no Wu by those names were found. Unable to effect service, the summonses were returned. Presiding Officer Gao then ruled that since the accuser Guan Tiren had already been questioned at the forensic office and no other credible evidence had emerged, and because the petitioners could not be summoned, the court could not proceed to reopen the coffin for further examination. The case was postponed pending further notice and the petitioners’ legal representatives would be informed.

Overloaded Cart Seized 165

Over 2,000 liang of opium seized

Value not less than 6,000 silver dollars

Yesterday at 3:30 p.m., a rickshaw driver, Zhang Fengxian of Jiangbei, was seen on Tiantong Road heading west with a heavily laden little cart. The No. 607 Chinese patrol officers suspected foul play, ordered him to stop for inspection, but the group escorting the cart menaced the officers. Fearing coercion, the patrol officers sounded an alarm whistle and displayed their sidearms, scattering the would-be obstructers. Nearby constables Nos. 1293 and 1299 arrived to assist, captured the escort Zhang Guiqing and Zhang Fengxian, and unloaded one of two wicker baskets on the cart, revealing opium earth in the other. The two were taken to the Hongkou Precinct, and the substance—2,160 liang—was weighed. At a market rate of three yuan per liang, its value was no less than 6,000 silver dollars. Yesterday morning, the two Zhangs and part of the contraband were sent to the Temporary Court for a hearing by Presiding Officer Wang Jianzu. According to the precinct captain, this vast quantity of opium earth belonged to an opium distributor. Their practice provided that any agent caught by authorities would, after serving his term, receive one yuan per day from the distributing company. The captain urged severe punishment as a deterrent. Under questioning, the Zhangs gave evasive answers, and Zhang Guiqing sought counsel. Presiding Officer Wang, after reviewing the testimony, sentenced Zhang Guiqing to two months’ imprisonment and a fine of 1,000 yuan, and Zhang Fengxian to two months’ imprisonment; the opium earth was confiscated.

Another World Tour Group Arrives in Shanghai 166

The American Second World Touring Party, with 335 members, arrived in Shanghai aboard the SS Queen of Australia of the Changxing Line. Having departed New York and visited Europe, they were due at Wusong at 7 a.m. today. The Changxing Company dispatched two tugboats to meet them at the New Customs Wharf. A large contingent of rickshaws and motorcars were engaged to transport the group around the city. They will return to the ship tonight and sail tomorrow to Qinhuangdao, to tour Beijing and Tianjin, then proceed to Japan and return to the United States via the Pacific–Panama Canal.

Fire in Guanghua University Women’s Dormitory Early This Morning 167

Aftermath measures decided

At about 8 a.m. yesterday, a sudden fire broke out in the women’s dormitory of Guanghua University on Daxi Road. The blaze was fierce. University authorities urgently telegraphed the fire brigade for assistance, but due to the distance, by the time engines arrived around 10 a.m. the fire had already spread uncontrollably. Though firefighters worked vigorously, they made little headway until after 10 a.m. When the fire began, most students were in class and no lives were lost, but, with nobody on-site to salvage belongings, all furnishings were destroyed. The loss is estimated at 20,000–30,000 yuan. Afterwards, the university convened an emergency meeting and decided to vacate some classrooms to serve as temporary dormitories for the women students, relocate faculty members from their quarters, and convert those into dormitories for the remainder of the term. All classes—male and female—will resume as normal next Monday. Meanwhile, additional funds have been allocated for the construction of a new women’s dormitory.

Effective from March 1

According to the Shanghai Times, the French Municipal Council has decided that bars and liquor outlets within the French Concession, like those in the International Settlement, may extend opening only until 4 a.m. on Saturdays and holidays; on all other days they must close by 2 a.m. The regulation will take effect on March 1 and has been formally promulgated.

According to registered hospital reports

The Municipal Health Bureau compiles monthly statistics on infectious diseases for the city government. Commission Disputes Among Firewood Dealers 172

According to the National News Agency, following a notice two years ago from the City Temple Firewood Industry Office increasing standard commissions by one fen, wood dealers at Xujiahui, Maple Forest Bridge, Panjiawood Bridge, and Rihui Bridge implemented the increase. However, dealers along Luban Bridge on Xielu Road, led by Wu Fuxing, charged commissions of five to seven fen. They recently cited rising expenses and higher living costs as justification for matching the official increase, though they still charged eight fen on rice straw. This was said to balance costs while showing consideration for customers. Unexpectedly, a dealer named Miao on Rihui Road sought to expand operations toward Luban Bridge, encouraging boatmen to oppose the nine-member coalition led by Wu Fuxing to raise commissions. The coalition immediately ceased supply in protest, reported the matter to the Second District Public Security Bureau, which detained Miao and ordered him to relocate his branch to avoid further disputes. It was noted that the increased commission did not exceed regulations, so their adjustment was permitted.

Report on Bandit Suppression off Wusongkou 173

Naval and water-police vessels set out to rescue the Zhejiang Fisheries Bureau patrol boat Haiying—which had been seized by pirates—patrolled the East China Sea about two hundred li east of Wusongkou, but the vastness of the waters made pursuit difficult. The water police then returned to patrol near Shengshan. Meanwhile, the naval gunboat Yongsheng remained in the East China Sea in pursuit. Yesterday at about 5 p.m., five or six large sailing junks loaded with pirates were spotted in Shengshan harbor, where they were looting several fishing vessels. The Water Police Fourth District Guerrilla Unit’s patrol boat Huhan happened upon them. Its commander Wang Qingfu ordered his crew to open fire, and the pirates returned fire. After more than an hour’s engagement, several cannon shots struck the pirate vessels, which abandoned the captured fishing boats and fled separately toward the East China Sea. The chase is ongoing. In the action, the Huhan patrol boat sustained several hits and six water police officers were wounded. Shengshan’s wireless station received the news and immediately relayed it to the Wusong Fort and Coastal Defense Office. Director Wu received the report and has dispatched vessels to assist in the suppression.

Empty Grounds in Hongkou Park Approved for Game Fields Rental 174

The Municipal Works Bureau hereby announces that, in response to requests from various quarters to lease vacant land within Hongkou Park for the organization of game fields, any group wishing to rent such space must submit a written application to this bureau for review. Once approved, the layout of the grounds will be publicly announced. All lessees must form a body, elect a responsible representative, and specify in the application: (1) the name of the organization; (2) the representative’s name; (3) the secretary’s name and address; (4) the number of members; (5) the desired location; (6) the months, dates, and times the space will be used; (7) whether they are willing to alternate usage days with other groups if demand exceeds supply; and (8) the location leased in the previous season. Applications must be submitted by Thursday, April 3, to the Director of Works for approval. The bureau will then allocate park land for use this season. This notice is hereby promulgated. Dated February 20, 1930. Chief Administrator Edward.

Debate in the Fraud Case of Tang Zhongze of the China Fuan Life Insurance Company 175

In the Temporary Court, Tang Zhongze, Chief Supervisor of the China Fuan Life Insurance Company, is charged with embezzlement. After multiple inquiries, Presiding Officer Gao reopened the hearing in Courtroom 14 yesterday. Several new plaintiffs, including Jiang Heshou, joined, represented by counsel Fei Guodao. After a concise statement by counsel, Judge Gao questioned the defendant briefly and proceedings moved to debate. Plaintiff’s counsel He Xianzhang argued that the Shanghai branch of China Fuan had no capital of its own and no paid-in stock. Since its parent, Hong Kong Fuan, suffered losses and could not continue life-insurance operations, its insurance business was transferred to the shell entity China Fuan to exploit the Hong Kong company’s license, Shanghai address, and existing staff, deceiving policyholders. In August of the sixteenth year, the Hong Kong head office announced a liquidation in Hong Kong newspapers, but Shanghai kept collecting premiums in secret until March of the eighteenth year, when it made a public liquidation announcement while claiming that China Fuan would continue operations. Internally, the two companies were one and the same, but externally both collected premiums; any claims were attributed to the purportedly liquidated Hong Kong company, which refused to pay. Counsel Fei added that after “liquidation,” all company assets and liabilities should have been turned over to the liquidator Yi Maoli. However, in August the defendant placed a notice in the paper declaring that his duties ended with liquidation, after which he privately sold company property for over 120,000 yuan and absconded with the funds. The company’s books for the seventeenth year show that on March 27, Tang Zhongze received 95,967 liang of spare funds and repaid himself 55,967 liang—indicating embezzlement. Premium collection and disbursement entries were all in his name, with no dealings with the Hong Kong company. Thus the Shanghai entity was a sham run by the defendant to defraud policyholders. Plaintiff’s counsel Yao Jiancha, Ren Weiyu, and others also spoke. Defense counsel Wang Shang countered that there was only one company; liquidating the Hong Kong parent while not liquidating the Shanghai branch was akin to the old Northern Exchange note suspension outside Beijing, with some branches still honoring notes. Recently the Hankou branch did the same. Hence Hong Kong Fuan’s liquidation was not illegal and could not form a criminal case. If the court deemed an audit necessary, it could refer the matter to a civil tribunal, to which he had no objection. The debate lasted about three hours and was unfinished when time ran out; it was adjourned to continue on the afternoon of the 27th, with a counterclaim also under investigation.

Judgment in the Impersonation Suit against the Zhou Huchen Brush Shop 176

Wang Chunjian expresses dissatisfaction

Official order allows appeal by law

In the sixteenth year of the Republic, Yangzhou native Wang Chunjian leased the trade name of the Jiangxi branch of the Zhou Huchen Brush Shop and set up a business in Xiangfen Alley off North Stone Road in Shanghai. His brushes bore the signature “Zhou Huchen Zhengji” in addition to the shop name. In the following January, the proprietor of the original Zhou Huchen shop in Xingsheng Street, Zhou Jinyun, sued him in the Temporary Court for impersonation. Wang voluntarily abandoned his claim and closed his shop, ending the lawsuit. Last winter, Wang’s brother Wang Chunhua carried the remaining brushes to a friend’s place but was seen by Wan En from Hongkou, who confiscated them and confronted Wang on grounds of impersonation. Wang resisted and both were taken to the Second District police station, then transferred to the District Court. After preliminary examination by Prosecutor Zhu and failure to reconcile, Wan filed criminal charges for impersonation and business obstruction, while Wang defended himself in multiple hearings. Wan first submitted four brushes made by Zhou Jinyun, then two imitation order forms, claiming they were found with the brushes. Wang argued that if the forms were found simultaneously, why were they not submitted earlier, only at trial, and that they were forged. After debate concluded the day before yesterday, the court sentenced Wang to two months’ imprisonment and a fine of 100 yuan. Wang immediately expressed dissatisfaction, and the official order permits him to appeal according to law.

Yuan Chaoxian Demands Money from Wife 177

Wife’s conduct too improper

Yuan uses excuse to slash her five times

Yuan Chaoxian, age thirty-nine, a tenant on the upper floor of Lin Yongmao’s firewood shop in Yangjiashi Bridge, Zhabei, formerly ran a flower-garden business and now idled at home. His wife, Miss Fan, age thirty-four and also from Yangzhou, known for her beauty, felt embarrassed by her husband’s humble appearance and sought an illicit liaison with a fellow townsman to obtain money. Yuan discovered the affair. Yesterday at 3:30 p.m., he demanded money from his wife; when she refused, he flew into a rage, seized a kitchen knife, and slashed her lower body five times. Only one cut to her right ankle reached about one inch deep, but it bled profusely. The station guard and constables arrived and arrested Yuan and nine others, taking them to the Fourth District Public Security Bureau for processing. After a preliminary hearing by the district chief, Yuan was transferred to the judicial division for severe punishment. The injured wife was sent to Baolong Hospital for treatment.

A Woman with Two Paramours 178

Madame Huang Wang, a thirty-seven-year-old woman from Yancheng, claimed there was no such thing as jealousy over infidelity. Two years ago last spring, because of her husband, she came to Shanghai with her nine-year-old son Daqing and found work in a silk factory in Zhabei to make a living. Although she was no longer young, she still possessed elegant charm and could not get used to sleeping alone. She therefore entered into an affair with her fellow townsman Wu Jizhou and established their nest in Pavilion No. 7 in Chongzhen Lane off Huasheng Road on Gonghe New Road. They shared the same bed as if husband and wife. After Madame Huang began her liaison with Wu, she abandoned her silk-factory work. All her daily expenses were provided by Wu, who worked as a partner in a sauce plantation in Zhabei and earned very little each month. Madame Huang often expressed dissatisfaction, and soon she also took up with Li Along, a fellow from Changshu who was partner in a tobacco shop. Without letting either man know of the other, she went back and forth between them; for over a year they never met face to face and all was quiet. At nine o’clock one morning, Li and Madame Huang were entwined in each other’s arms asleep when Wu suddenly returned home. Bursting into the room, he lifted the curtain and was shocked to see Madame Huang lying side by side with Li. Burning with fury, he grabbed the two of them, intending to take them outside to report them to the police. But Madame Huang threw herself between him and his captives, seized his hands, and declared that both men were her paramours, that there was no issue of jealousy over infidelity, and that they could talk it over. Wu’s anger only grew upon hearing this. He then seized Li and brutally beat him. Madame Huang rushed to Li’s aid and joined in beating Wu. Overpowered by two against his one, Wu released his hold and fled outside to report the matter to the relevant police station, requesting their arrest and interrogation.

Tryst of Three Men and Four Women 179

Mrs. Shen and Mrs. Zhang Seduce a Maiden

Xu Abao’s Vanity Leads to Loss of Virtue

Xu Shen, a forty-six-year-old woman from Songjiang, had a daughter named Abao, now eighteen years old, whose appearance was not unattractive. The previous year, they lived in their hometown, but two springs ago Madame Xu brought her daughter to Shanghai and they both worked as domestic help for a family on Shengyi Lane, No. 4 in the South City. Because the work was extremely busy, mother and daughter resigned together and later Madame Xu went to work for the Shen family on Dandongmen Tangjia Lane. But Abao, temporarily without an employer, was entrusted to a matchmaker surnamed Zhu on Huay Street to find work. One afternoon while waiting in Zhu’s shop, she was approached by her acquaintance Mrs. Shen Zhang from Pinghu, who, under the pretext of taking supper, invited Abao and two other women out to visit the Little World amusement park. There they encountered three men known to Mrs. Shen. In the course of conversation, one man surnamed Zhao became particularly intimate with Abao. After their tour ended, the group of seven went to lodge at a hotel in the North City. During that time, Zhao privately told Abao that her life as domestic help was too hard, and she would be better off living with him. He said he was employed by a foreign firm with a hefty monthly income; if she agreed, she would never worry about food or clothing again. He even promised to buy her a fashionable qipao the next day. Abao, a country girl new to Shanghai, was flattered and moved by his words and fell into his scheme. That night she shared his bed and was raped. Madame Shen slept with another man, while the remaining two women shared a bed with the third man. After the night passed, Abao and the three men arranged to meet again later and then parted. Madame Shen accompanied Abao back to the matchmaker’s shop. The matchmaker, alarmed that Abao had been out all night and had not returned, immediately notified Xu Shen, who came to the shop to question her. At first Abao, ashamed, remained silent, but after her mother pressed her repeatedly she disclosed the details. On hearing of her daughter’s prior betrothal to a man in her hometown and how she had been lured out and defiled by Mrs. Shen, Xu Shen was furious. She immediately took Abao to capture Mrs. Shen and summoned Watchman Liu Shaoqing of the Seventh Post to bring her to the First District Substation. Under preliminary questioning by the superintendent, orders were given to detain them pending further investigation.

Rape, Abduction of a Girl, and Attempted Resale 180

Yan Lamei, a seventeen-year-old girl from Pudong, was lured and abducted to the home of An Shiqing in South Nanyang Lane, French Concession vegetable market street, where she was forcibly raped and kept. An then conspired with Song Jialin to try to sell her. The detective Wang Dongwan of the French Police Station learned of the incident and arrested An and Song. Yan Lamei was taken together into custody and brought before the French magistrate’s court for prosecution. Under interrogation, Yan Lamei stated that she had been living as a daughter-in-law in a certain household but had fled due to cruel treatment by her mother-in-law. On the road she met An, who tricked her into his home and raped her several times before conspiring with Song to sell her. Upon questioning, An Shiqing admitted to the abduction and rape but denied any intent to sell. Song Jialin testified that recently a civilian passing by An’s door had been invited inside, and at that moment the detective arrived to arrest them; he gave evasive answers to other questions. The French magistrate sentenced An Shiqing to five years in the Western jail, Song Jialin to one year as a warning to others, and ordered Yan Lamei sent to a relief shelter to await family claim.

Lu Baosheng Seeks to Disown His Unworthy Son 181

Lu Baosheng, a man from Pudong working as a clerk at Changtai Rice Shop on Dou Market Street in the South City, lived at Cangqiao. His son, nicknamed Azhi, aged twenty-two, had apprenticed in the rice business but had been idle since the shop closed two years ago. He associated with ruffians and secretly pawned household items for his own use. His father repeatedly admonished him to no avail and was met with insults. Recently, Azhi began demanding money from his father and beating him when his demands were not met. Relatives, unable to bear his conduct, expelled him from the house. But Azhi continued to extort his father outside. Lu, upon receiving an apology from Azhi for his behavior, was enraged and contemplated drowning himself to avoid further burdening his family. Relatives dissuaded him. He now intends to file a formal statement with the authorities to sever all relations with his son.

Victims of Economic Oppression 182

Jiang Wenbing Dies of Hydrogen Peroxide Poisoning

Jiang Wenbing, a thirty-eight-year-old Zhejiang native living at No. 201 Tongqing Lane on Yuezhou Road, had formerly worked as a manager at the CSSC Jinliyuan Wharf warehouse with a substantial monthly income. During the May Third Movement sit-down strikes, he ceased working and in the ensuing years fell into financial distress. He had repeatedly contemplated suicide but was rescued each time. Recently, through a friend’s introduction he joined the Dalai Company in Pudong as a dockworker but earned very little, struggling to make ends meet. Plagued by constant worry, he secretly ingested an entire bottle of hydrogen peroxide yesterday around 5 p.m. Colleagues discovered him and immediately arranged a boat to send him to Renji Hospital for treatment, but he died at 9:30 p.m. that night.

Soldier Jumps from Train and Dies 183

Yesterday afternoon at 3:15, Train No. 8 from Shanghai to Hangzhou on the Shanghai–Hangzhou Railway carried a soldier of the Fifth Division, boarding at Longhua. When the train passed Meijia Lane Station, which it does not normally stop at, the soldier suddenly jumped from the carriage and fell, sustaining fatal injuries. He died on the spot before medical help could arrive. The stationmaster dispatched police to handle the matter and notified the local garrison. The unit sent a platoon leader and several soldiers to the station with a coffin to retrieve the body.

Chen Asi’s Rice Boat Capsizes in the Huangpu 184

Hongji Rice Merchant in Shanghai, having recently purchased five hundred dan of polished rice at Wujiang Lili in the interior, hired two boats—Bao Bao and Chen As Five—to transport it to Shanghai. Chen As Five, however, was a native riverboat unfamiliar with Shanghai’s waterways, so it followed the Bao boat. Two days ago, both boats set sail with fair winds and approached the South Huangpu docks. When the wind suddenly turned strong, the Bao boat skillfully turned its rudder and sped ahead, separating by about a li from the Chen boat. Within moments, the Chen boat disappeared from view. Fearing the Chen crew’s unfamiliarity with the route, the Bao crew anchored and waited, but by sunset the Chen boat had not reappeared. At dawn the next day, they returned to the docks but found no trace of the Chen boat. Inquiring of other moored vessels, they learned that a cargo boat had capsized in Liuhang Bank due to careless navigation and wind-driven waves, and the hull could be seen just protruding above the waterline. They recognized it as the Chen boat and, as the crew on shore called for help, anchored nearby and inquired about what had happened. They were told that a delayed rudder turn amid the sudden emergency had caused the boat to capsize. Two of Chen’s crew fell into the water and were missing; another crew member was battered by the waves onto a sandbank but was rescued by a passing vessel and brought to shore, where his clothes were changed. The Bao crew took their injured colleague back aboard their boat; both were severely hurt and could not walk. The Bao boat then took them by motorcar to Shanghai for medical treatment. It was reported that Chen’s boat carried 180 dan of rice, and the loss was substantial. Salvage operations for the sunken cargo are underway.

German Veteran Dentist Suffers Mental Breakdown and Dies 185

Dr. Maspei, a sixty-four-year-old German dentist with over thirty years’ practice and considerable reputation in Shanghai, was famed for his fondness of drinking, often spending his days in a tipsy haze like a European Liu Ling. His residence was at No. 350 Weihaiwei Road in the Western Concession. At around 9 p.m. on the 15th of this month, the inebriated dentist left a drinking house in Hongkou and, as he made his way home on Tongfu Road, lost consciousness and strength, collapsing beside the road. Officers from the Xinzha Police Station (No. 1,263) found him, too drunk to speak, stinking of alcohol. They summoned a carriage to take him to Gongji Hospital for treatment, where he died on the afternoon of the 18th. Since his residence fell under the jurisdiction of the Jing’an Temple Police Station, that station ordered his body taken to the forensic institute on Philbin Road. Yesterday morning, Judge Luo Renji of the Provisional Court examined the body and, after convening a hearing, ruled the death a natural demise due to mental illness. Witnesses linked to the police stated that the deceased had been old and had a history of mental disorder, having fallen twice before. From his admittance to the hospital until his death, he did not speak a word. The police had no suspicion of foul play. As Dr. Maspei had no family in Shanghai, the station notified the German consulate to have his remains collected and buried by consular staff.

Municipal Bureau Garbage Truck Kills Apprentice 186

At about 3 p.m. two days ago, Municipal Bureau garbage truck No. 5552 on Beinanhe Road Bridge struck and killed a boy of about sixteen or seventeen. Yesterday the corpse was removed to the forensic institute on Philbin Road by police from the Customs House Station, and the Provisional Court appointed Judge Luo Renji to investigate. He determined the boy was run over and fatally injured by the truck. According to testimony by Inspector Zhong Lawen, the truck was traveling north when the boy dashed across the road east–west in front of it. The driver saw him, stopped the engine, but the front of the truck then hit a telegraph pole and the rear wheels ran over the boy. Witness Mei Guangfu, a clerk at Wantai Lumber, said he heard a loud noise and saw the truck hit the pole, then saw a person knocked down. The driver, Zhou Songgui, testified he was carrying garbage north from the Henan Road Bridge when the boy ran from west to east. The road was narrow and he applied the brake but could not avoid striking the pole, and the wheels then ran over the victim. He pleaded for understanding. A woman, Mrs. Chen Wang of No. 98, Lane 9, Guangyu Circle on Tiantong Road, reported that the deceased was her husband Chen Agen’s apprentice, nicknamed Little Chongming, seventeen years old; his father works at a sauce factory on Chongming Road. She could not decide on the matter and asked his father to handle it. Judge Luo ordered the body to remain at the forensic institute for three days pending relatives’ identification. If no one appears, the body will be delivered to the Pu Shan Shan Zhuang for burial. As the driver’s negligence could not be conclusively proven, he was released. Because the victim was poor, the court advised the Municipal Bureau to grant compensation.

Pig Trader Kidnapped at Baimaosha 187

The Shanghai Dunren Office received a report from pig-boat owner Lu that pig buyers Ji Qi, Wu Deyuan, and others were kidnapped last night at the Baimaosha area. The office immediately notified the District Four Waterborne Public Security Bureau to pursue and rescue them.

Public Security Bureau District Two Precinct One Captures Highway Robbers 188

Five Arrested in the French Concession

All Three Bandits Have Confessed

Near the western end of Zhangchuan Lane in the South City’s Little World area, at around 9 p.m. on the 11th of this month, a highway robbery occurred in which the victim Wang Nengwei was shot and wounded by the thieves. After the incident, Chief Qian of Precinct One in District Two immediately ordered his subordinates to intensify the search and ensure the perpetrators were apprehended. On the 17th, a tip from an informant reported that the main suspects were hiding in Room 25 of the Chunjiang Hotel at No. 309 Caishi Street in the French Concession. Acting on the tip, Chief Qian dispatched Inspectors Jiang and Qiang, along with several officers and informants. They first obtained authorization from the French Police Station to carry out the arrest. The station head then sent detectives to accompany them to the address, where they indeed arrested the suspects—three men and two women—and seized one pistol, two bullets, fourteen promissory notes, and an IOU beneath a table. They were taken to the station for temporary detention. Yesterday, Inspector Jiang presented the five suspects, along with the weapon and other items, at the French magistrate’s court. Under questioning by Chief Qian, one confessed to being named Pan Amao, 25, from Pudong, living at Yangjiazhai; another admitted to being Ai Guizhong, 31, from Zhenjiang, residing at 109 Rongsheng Lane, Xinzhaqiao; a third, Yang Chang—31, from Changzhou, residing at No. 14 Qiao Lane; and the woman identified herself as Zhang Xiaowan, 23, from Pudong, working at Lin’s. Another suspect, 31-year-old from Nanjing living at No. 21 Zhangjiabang, also confessed. All five have unequivocally admitted their crimes.

Minister Wang Advocates "Shuowen Gulin" 190

Wang Gongxi, minister to Japan, is a passionate student of Chinese studies, especially philology and phonology. Having recently read Mr. Ding Fubao’s compilation "Shuowen Gulin" from Wuxi, he published an essay recommending it to scholars of philology. He notes that "Shuowen" is the oldest and most comprehensive Chinese dictionary; anyone wishing to understand character formation, ancient pronunciations, and meanings should study it. In recent Qing scholarship, textual criticism has flourished. Confucian scholars regard exegesis as the gateway to moral cultivation and therefore hold works on "Shuowen" in as high esteem as classics. Those specializing in "Shuowen" have produced countless volumes, but few could read them all. The minister had long aspired to compile a concise commentary on "Shuowen" by selecting essentials from Qing scholars, but could not complete it. Now, upon reading Mr. Ding Zhonghu’s "Shuowen Gulin," which gathers 180 works across more than 1,000 volumes from Xiao Xu to Qing scholars like Zang Yulin, and organizes and annotates every entry with cross-references, he realized his dream fulfilled by Mr. Ding’s thirty years of effort. Future scholars will greatly benefit from this compendium, saving enormous time and labor. The minister urges all with interest in classical studies to seek out this book.

Zhejiang’s Premier Teahouse Opens New Reading Room 191

Since opening last year at the intersection of Zhejiang Road and Ningbo Road, Zhejiang No. 1 Teahouse has been thriving. To cater to the intellectual community, it has now established an exquisitely furnished reading room on the second floor, hiring renowned artists from Guangyu Society for live music and singing. Printing Company Sues Tobacco Company for Unpaid Debts 194

Jurisdictional Issue Concerning Ling Runxiang

Hearing Postponed, Witnesses to Be Resummoned

After the collapse of Huamin Tobacco Company, owing substantial debts, the company engaged Lawyer Wu Chengyu to settle affairs. On March 25 last year, Wu convened a creditors’ meeting and voted to pay half of the debts in full settlement. However, Greater China Printing Company, owed 4,513 yuan, received nothing. Lawyer Yang Guoshu was retained to sue Huamin and its shareholders, including Ling Runxiang and Rong Jieqing, at the Provisional Court for full repayment. The plaintiff’s investigation located Ling at No. 158 Taihua Lane on Beijiangxi Road, but Rong’s address was unknown. The court issued summons; on the afternoon of the day before yesterday, court officers, accompanied by Lawyer Yang, went to serve Ling. A young woman inside said her husband Ling Runxiang was away. Yesterday the case was heard by Judge Ye. Ling, represented by Attorney Yan, raised a jurisdictional objection, arguing he now worked for the Public Security Bureau and resided in the Concession, so the case should be transferred. A Guangdong youth surnamed Zheng testified that the property had been transferred to Ling in November last year. Under cross-examination, Zheng’s statements proved unreliable. Judge Ye ordered the hearing postponed pending further summons of witnesses, including Ling himself, whose current address is confirmed at Beijiangxi Road where he serves in the Public Security Bureau.

French Classic "La Dame aux Camélias" Reprinted and Selling Well 196

"The Lady of the Camellias," the famous love story by Alexandre Dumas fils, has delighted readers worldwide. After Lin Qinnan’s Chinese translation, a local poet lamented "the lamentable Camellias, destroying every Chinese playboy’s soul." Unfortunately, Lin’s version was abridged and altered, losing much of the original’s charm. Last year, Mr. Xia Kangneng produced a new translation faithful to the original in fluent, elegant prose, creating a sensation among readers. A deluxe reprint is now selling briskly. "Manon Lescaut," another celebrated French romance—whose screen adaptation "Manon Lescaut" draws on it—will also soon be reprinted. Both books are published by the Spring Tide Bookstore in the Central Market on Malu Road.
