The Efficacy of "Yin-Yang Vine" 198

"Yin-Yang Vine," the miraculous remedy for pulmonary tuberculosis, is produced by the Eastern Pharmaceutical Company at No. 64 Songshan Road. Since its release, it has cured many lives. Whether bleeding cough, tubercular phthisis, or chronic or acute coughs, treatment with this vine achieves complete recovery—two doses for mild cases and half a catty for serious ones—effectively eradicating the disease. If it fails, the company offers double the refund. As a result, business has been booming along Shanyin Road, with demand far outstripping supply. The price is $2 per dose or $20 per catty, and major pharmacies outside the concession also offer it on consignment.

Incoming Correspondence

Notice to the Far Eastern Opium Investigation Delegation 207

Luo Yunyan

The League of Nations has appointed a committee to investigate and report on opium suppression in the Far East. Their mandate includes: (1) the status of opium smoking in each country consenting to this investigation; (2) each government’s performance in implementing Chapter II of the 1912 Hague Opium Convention and the 1925 Geneva Opium Convention; (3) the conditions of opium smuggling in Far Eastern countries, the difficulties of prohibition, and plans for future enforcement.

According to Article II of the 1925 Convention, exporting nations must first prohibit the export of raw opium to prevent the spread of opium‑related harm to consuming countries; secondly, they must intensify domestic suppression of opium smoking, aiming for total abolition within fifteen years of the final resolution of the League’s Advisory Committee on the Traffic in Opium and Other Dangerous Drugs. By this measure, the success or failure of opium suppression policies in the Far East depends almost entirely on the Committee. Hence, the Committee’s mission is both weighty and important.

However, we cannot help but raise doubts about the formation of this Committee and cannot but oppose its intentions. We therefore present the following points for the public’s information. (To be continued)

The Arts

The Recent Czechoslovak Literary Scene 210

— Ming

In the view of most non‑Slavic peoples, the representatives of modern Czechoslovak literature are František Langer and Karel Čapek. In fact, however, this is not the case. Individually, they are indeed outstanding figures in modern Czech letters, but their influence on the broader Czech literary movement is quite limited. Since Čapek’s last play, “The Creator Adam,” received a lukewarm reception, he has only published one short‑story collection and spends most of his time gardening for amusement and writing a daily column on personalities and events for Lidové noviny (one of the Czech Republic’s leading newspapers). As for Langer, his recent comedy, “Štěpánkovy Proměny” (“The Transformation of Private Štěpánek”), has achieved considerable success, humorously portraying a hotel porter converted into a Salvation Army believer by a Salvation Army lass. Yet this play is not a truly significant work and does not match his masterpiece, “The Periphery.” Therefore, neither is a central figure on today’s Czech literary scene. Beyond them, many important Czech novelists, poets, and dramatists remain little known abroad due to the lack of translations of their works.

Czechoslovak literature, like the country itself, is in a period of transition. In life, commerce, literature, art, and architecture, it continues to absorb foreign cultures without yet creating its own. The book market overflows with translations of major modern works from England, America, France, Germany, Russia, and the Nordic countries. The prewar protest literature movement has lost all justification and standing. As for the new national literary movement, it has not yet taken firm root, though several clearly defined groups are creating in a modified nationalist vein. The most prominent of these is the Legionary faction, so called because its writers all served on the front lines during the Great War.

War has always been—and remains—a primary theme for Czech novelists. Their war narratives are not abstract but realistic accounts of the Czech army’s experiences in Russia and Serbia. The leading writers in this faction are Joseph Kopta and Jaroslav Kratochvíl. Kopta has just completed the third volume of his trilogy; the first two depict the Czechs’ harrowing battles against the Bolsheviks and their tragic march through Siberia to Vladivostok. The third volume, titled “The Third Regiment’s Return Home,” portrays the disillusionment of the troops upon returning to their newly freed homeland. Kopta’s work is highly authentic; he truly voices the soldiers. Kratochvíl, by contrast, leans more toward the artist and interpreter of events: his “The Path of Revolution” is a tragedy of the Siberian campaign. He is now writing a novel also set in Siberia, not yet fully published, but from the released portions it promises to become a truly great Czech novel.

To understand the multifaceted state of modern Czechoslovak literature, one must examine the personality of Jaroslav Durych. His poems, dramas, novels, essays, and pamphlets reveal his diverse nature. This Roman Catholic “enfant terrible” is a prolific writer. Having recently published the poetry collection “Peninky” (“Country Maidens”) and the novel “Na Hrach” (“On the Heights”), he followed with the three‑volume biographical novel “Bloudení” (“Error”), a detailed study of Count Wallenstein, the famed general of the Thirty Years’ War.

Karel J. Beneš’s novel “Rudé Léto” (“Red Summer”) is the first installment of an epic work depicting an entire era, aimed at portraying postwar Czech society in its entirety. “Red Summer” reaches up to the summer of 1920, when the Bolsheviks threatened the Czechoslovak border; the panic and confusion in Czech society at that time are vividly portrayed. Emil Vachek is also a prolific author, artist, and journalist, renowned as a naturalist best at writing gritty realist novels about life at society’s lowest levels. His recent novel “Parasites” provides a detailed portrayal of the lives of postwar social parasites. Jaroslav Maria likewise writes about social life, focusing on pessimistic and pathological studies, evident in his three‑volume novel “Orlové a Stržílci” (“The Eagle and the Wren”). A. Longen’s novel “Herečka” (“Actress”) is of great value to those interested in Czech art and theater life, as it tells the story of his wife, a modernist actress. These are all important novelists.

As for literary criticism—especially of the scholarly variety—Czechoslovakia is by no means lacking. Since the death of the country’s greatest poet, Otakar Březina, Czech criticism has become lively. Many books have been published studying and appreciating this great poet. The two most noteworthy are “Conversations on Otakar Březina,” one by Mrs. Pjickova Saupkova, Březina’s best German translator and wife, and the other by Jakub Deml, a key figure in the Czech Catholic revival and close friend to poets. The recorded conversations of these poets are full of interest and expressive power. In the current critical arena, František Goetz is the most important representative of the Czech youth; he will soon publish a collection of critiques titled “Tvar Století” (“The Face of the Age”) discussing the main tendencies of contemporary writers. Karl Tiege is the sharpest and most intelligent theorist of the radical modernist movement in literature and art; he has two new works: “On Humor, Clowns, and Dadaists” and “Modern Architecture in Czechoslovakia.”

Modern poetry is wholly lyrical, and is being pursued entirely by a group of young writers. Prewar veterans have either died or ceased writing. In Czech poetry the French influence is very evident. Among the younger poets there are no truly great figures; comparatively, Vítězslav Nezval, Jaroslav Seifert, and Hora are the most important.

Stimulation Beauty and Dissonance Beauty 211

— Ri Kui

(In every era and every social stratum, at each stage of its historical development, people have discovered and created various forms of beauty. Sometimes what they unearthed was the beauty of the human figure, the beauty of still life; at other times it was the beauty of heroic tragedy, erotic beauty, even the beauty of the monstrous. Some of these beauties vanished with their era; others transformed their appearance. Today, the beauties of the metropolis and of machinery are reflected in modern art.)

(The above passage is quoted from a discussion in “The Search for New Artistic Forms,” by the Japanese expert on proletarian literary theory, Kura Hara Koreto (藏原惟人). It is introduced here as this article’s opening statement, to indicate that the criterion of beauty lies in its mobility. Especially in modern literary and artistic works, beyond extolling the beauties of city life and machinery, we have lately discovered stimulation beauty, dissonance beauty, barbaric beauty, and even the beauty of Black civilization, and so forth. Putting aside for the moment the “beauty of barbarism” and the “beauty of Black civilization,” let us first examine the issues of stimulation beauty and dissonance beauty.)

The standard of beauty is dynamism rather than fixed immutability. Whether its shifts are gradual or precipitous, in sum there is no absolutely fixed criterion of beauty.

Broadly speaking, beauty may be divided into two main types: active beauty and static beauty. The former advances by engaging with existing life; the latter is governed solely by the human mind and heart. Among active beauties, none is more vivid and vigorous than stimulation beauty. Its intensity is the most fiery, truly the apex of modern beauty.

Eastern life and thought have always valued stability and tranquility; thus in their arts they have confined themselves to static beauty. Western life and thought inherit the traditions of Greek art and culture; hence everything strives toward fervor and upheaval. Their aesthetic ideal of life is extreme fluctuation and unrest; their art’s beauty is keen sensitivity and stimulating sharpness.

Putting aside now the abstract, hollow lecturing-style text,

Page 20

we turn to concrete evidence demonstrating how stimulation beauty affects modern life.

Open any daily newspaper and one third of it is taken up by full‑page cinema advertisements. A survey of the films showing at Shanghai’s more than thirty theaters finds that not a single one fails to tout slogans like “Romantic Carnality” or “Alluring Stimulation.” After all, going to the movies represents a type of modern stimulating life: for a modest sum, in one or two hours you can witness countless stirring, tragic, hilarious or poignant life tableaux. Tell me, of the audience seated before the silver screen, who does not go in search of spiritual excitement and neural stimulation?

Consider too the wildly popular football matches, horse races, and dog races—these frenzied sports that embellish metropolitan life. Which of them does not offer its own thrilling stimulus?

With coffeehouses and dance halls mushrooming everywhere, it need hardly be said that they are all intensely stimulating venues. Garden‑style tea salons and halls for storytelling accompanied by singing will gradually be phased out. What is now in vogue is the Café‑Restaurant, where charming young women serve and Jazz music delivers rapid, forceful melodies, accompanied by lively, wind‑swept dance stars. In short, every enjoyment in life today expresses active beauty and stimulation beauty.

Now a few words on dissonance beauty. Past art and aesthetic standards favored harmony and equilibrium as their two great characteristics. Since the collapse of feudalism, classical‑school art has likewise become bankrupt. Modern artists cannot enjoy an ordered, stable life; none have leisure to labor over works of harmony and balance. Thus dissonance beauty emerged in art at just the right moment.

When an artistic culture reaches full ripeness, it gradually leans toward dissonance—this is a natural phenomenon in historical progress.

Classical music’s essence is harmony; modern music has taken a different course, favoring chaotic cacophonies. It casts aside traditional compositional rules and introduces the savage, stimulating strains of folk melodies. This is one of the chief manifestations of dissonance beauty in modern art.

In painting, refined color palettes have been abandoned in favor of pure primary hues—crimson, azure, yellow, white, black—colors rich with intense stimulation, eagerly used by contemporary painters. In women’s fashion, too, we see the surging power of pure, stimulating hues: black velvet cheongsams, flesh‑colored stockings, pale foundation powders, dark brows, vivid rouges—all reveal a trend toward simplification, stimulation, and thus dissonance beauty.

The most conspicuous examples of dissonance beauty in women’s adornment are three fashionable trends: bobbed hair, bare feet, and the half‑stocking craze that swept the winter season.

Those who study modern art must in no way overlook these two great tendencies of stimulation beauty and dissonance beauty.

This is the conclusion of this essay.

Biography Literature in Demand 212

— Yan Gu

Recent Trends in Publishing

Lately the publishing world has seen a strong preference among general readers for works recounting the experiences and merits of great figures and celebrities. Next in popularity are light‑interest pieces in general news magazines—discussions of fathers, tributes to husbands, introductions to a wife’s half‑concealed life. Autobiographies and critical biographies of modern famous industrial magnates and politicians—such as Edison, Wanamaker, Ford, and Mussolini—are published in abundance, and their readership grows daily. Recent plans to reserve publication space for Baroudack’s heroic biographies, and Mr. Sugimori Kōjirō’s manuscript on the social significance of hero worship in the new era, are ironclad proof of the modern public’s hero‑worship fervor.

The distinctive feature of biographical literature is its grounding in the real life circumstances of its subjects. When introducing a certain type of figure, it fully recounts the era and spirit from which that person arose. Thus, by reading biographies of great and heroic individuals from ancient times to the present, one can gain insight into world history. Conversely, having a grasp of world history allows one to understand fully the deeds of these great worthies. Young readers who find biographies engaging do so because they help them master a wealth of social knowledge and cultivate elevated sentiments. In Europe and America, collections of anecdotes about statesmen and soldiers from the World War, supplemented with causes of the war and impressions, are published as biographical literature. In lands ravaged by war, reconstruction of society and the revival of industry everywhere demand the efforts of a conscious populace. The emotional and intellectual stimulus that readers receive from biographies of these great personages is not hard to imagine. In our recent age of democratically minded crowds and group consciousness, the sudden surge in hero worship may seem, at first glance, like a chronological misstep. Yet seeing that many of the populace prepare to adopt the thoughts and achievements of past heroes as the guiding lights for forging a new life, one can readily understand this phenomenon with a clear conscience.

Moreover, as everyone’s life becomes more mechanized and ordinary, their psychological longing for heroic achievements grows ever more unbreakable. Consider the United States, which achieved independence not long ago and seeks to excel in everything; this is but another aspect of the foregoing psychology.

Rudyard Kipling’s Commemorative Bust 213

— Mo Li

Rudyard Kipling, the British author one year senior to Minister Bishijin, is now very famous. Yet the name “Kipling” originated during his early years in Lahore, where he contributed to a magazine called The Home and the Army. By way of commemoration, a finely crafted marble bust has been sent to his former residence in Lahore.

Tales of World Literature (No. 140) 214

— John Mason, U.S.A.; translated by Zhong Hu

Chapter 37: Victorian‑Era Poetry

Looking back at Victorian poetry, we are astonished at its richness—even before we have named specific works. Yet without the greater siblings who ennobled literature—those poets better known as humorists than as poets, who wrote only three or four complete songs; poets like Thomas Hood; those who introduced Celtic magic, such as the Irish “Dark Rosaline” poet Mangan; poets like Clough, a friend of Arnold, who wrote sparse but truly lyrical poems; Covendry Patmore, author of tear‑jerking “Toy”‑like verses; Sidney Dobell, who wrote a beautiful vesper hymn before the advent of “free verse”; longer poems often in dialect, and short lyric pieces in classical English that, in thought, feeling, originality of courage, and melodic structure, were notable, such as those by island poet Thomas Edward Brown; and the brisk verse writers Landor and Dobson, among others.

From the late nineteenth century into the early twentieth century, there arose young poets who maintained the great tradition of English balladry while clearly adding individual tunes of life and death. To enumerate all their names is impossible. Among them were Oscar Wilde, author of the powerful “Ballad of Reading Gaol”; John Davidson, confirmed writer of robust folk songs; William B. Yeats, gifted with Celtic enchantment; A. E. Housman, whose “To an Athlete Dying Young” offers proof that a poet’s greatness cannot be measured by volume alone; John Masefield, master of lyric verse and adventure tales; W. B. Yeats; and others. The glory of English poetry persists not only in great solo voices but also in the chorus of smaller songsters—each a soloist—since poets are not choristers. Even in this brief outline, though unnamed, their works could form a fine anthology of beautiful poems.

There is consensus among living poets that the name Francis Thompson stands as the crowning peak of nineteenth‑century verse. He is not the last great English poet—there is no such thing here and there ought not to be—but we may, if not on critical grounds then on poetic grounds, regard him as a magnificent sunset and place the rich illusions of his “Sunset Hymn” upon his own brow:

Ah, you exiled sun,

Withdrawing each day your splendor!

How grand was your oriental rising!

Now you set in more august beauty,

Your peal resounds in heaven’s tumult,

Your cymbals thunder the western sky ablaze.

How composedly you die!

I but see your brass‑bright horns resound.

Gongdelin to Begin Scripture Lectures Tomorrow

Gongdelin Vegetarian Store announces that layperson Wang Yiting and others in Shanghai, using Elder Lingfeng’s commentary on the Amitābha Sūtra, will expound each phrase with insight and each word with sincerity. Illustrating principles to clarify mind and form, and relating stories to speak from the heart, they will dispel the illusions of conditional references, advocate subjective idealism, and offer clear evidence of the Nine Realms as the bridge to the Other Shore. Believing newcomers may find the material hard to grasp, they have specially invited Master Jingquan to lecture, beginning tomorrow (the 22nd) from 4:00 to 6:00 PM daily in the Great Hall of Gongdelin on Beijing Road. Master Jingquan, trained in the Tian Tai tradition and renowned for his unfettered eloquence, is highly esteemed in Buddhist studies. Interested listeners are expected in great numbers.

China Table Tennis Company announces that previously, all practice and competition balls were imported, causing troubling outflow of foreign currency. Theater News 216

Grand Bright opens “Laughing in the Face of Death” Starring Harold Lloyd

The films of comedic king Harold Lloyd have long delighted Shanghai audiences, but a talking picture has been lacking—until now. By sparing no expense, the Paramount Company in America has shipped “Laughing in the Face of Death,” Lloyd’s first full‑dialogue release, to our shores. He plays a youthful botanist turned amateur detective, whose mishaps are uproariously comic and whose resourceful agility astonishes as he darts about like a dragon flying with precision. Opposite him is American beauty and pageant winner Mrs. Blanche Glaum, his lovestruck heroine, in tender, sensuous romantic scenes. The film opens day and night beginning Friday.

Bright‑Land opens “Laughing in the Face of Death” Today

The Bright‑Land Grand Theater on the Museum Road announces that starting this afternoon, day and night screenings will begin of comedic king Harold Lloyd’s sole masterpiece “Laughing in the Face of Death.” For enhanced viewing, they have installed a brand‑new, enlarged screen and optics, making visuals more imposing and sound exceptionally clear. The film, shot over more than a year and more than 12,000 feet in twelve reels, is unparalleled among comedic pictures, featuring complete dialogue and Lloyd’s famous cough, long awaited by fans. The plot sweeps away today’s slapdash conventions, blending romance, humor, supernatural thrills, and danger with natural, coherent artistry—truly a great work years in the making. Leading lady Blanche Glaum, winner of the 1929 young actress award, delivers a voice of exquisite warmth and realism. Lloyd sought her personally to fill the role, reflecting her value. The cast also includes a kindly, devoted Chinese doctor and villainous Western scoundrels, their contrasts strikingly original. The entire film packs tremendous stimulation, balancing comedy and romance in an extraordinary talkie. To ensure good seats, call 19208 in advance to avoid overcrowding.

Social News

U.S. Building War‑Convertible Liners 217

World News Agency, Washington—The Atlantic shipping lanes long dominated by Britain and Germany have, over recent years, seen the U.S. actively expand its maritime power to compete with the Anglo‑German United Mail Steamship Company. A major shipbuilding plan has just been announced: two liners of over 50,000 tons each, 962 feet in length, and capable of 28 km/h—unmatched by any current vessels. Each will cost about US$30 million, and their internal appointments will rival the finest ocean‑liners for luxury, approaching land‑based hotel standards. Most noteworthy is their war‑ship–style construction, allowing rapid conversion into fully equipped warships if needed. Britain’s naval supremacy rests not on sheer quantity of warships but on its fleet of merchant vessels convertible to warships. Now the U.S. pursues the same strategy with these new liners—no mystery as to their purpose.

New York’s Tallest Buildings 218

New York’s tallest and most solidly built structure is the Chrysler Building, with seventy‑five floors and 850 feet in height. Yet the Empire State Building under construction on the old site of the Waldorf‑Astoria Hotel will be greater and taller: eighty‑five floors, about 970 feet high. In a few months, work will begin on The National City Bank & Farmers Trust Co. Building, a seventy‑one‑story, 925‑foot tower. Meanwhile, Metro‑Bolton Life Insurance Company plans a 100‑story tower near Madison Square, though its final height remains undecided. Finally, the Lefcourt Building, destined to be New York’s next tallest, is planned for Times Square at 1,050 feet.

Many engineers have opined that buildings should not exceed forty stories, yet today seventy‑ and eighty‑story structures are no novelty. In the coming years, a new record of one‑hundred‑story buildings may well be set; decades hence, towers over a hundred stories could become commonplace.

This is an undeniable fact: the drive to build taller stems not from engineering convenience but from economic factors. In New York City, land costs over US$200 per square foot—an astounding figure.

— Liang Xiongwan

Seventeen‑Year Comparison of U.S. Gold and Silver Coin Output 219

Amid rising gold prices, the world’s monetary situation warrants close attention, especially that of the United States, deemed the world’s wealthiest nation. By the end of September 1929, its national banks held US$4,008 million—an astonishing sum by Chinese standards. China’s economy lies largely in foreign hands, and in 1929 imports of raw silver reached 13.667 million ounces, driving domestic silver prices to a disastrous low. To assess whether China’s silver slump resulted from over‑production, we consult the latest seventeen‑year comparison report from the U.S. Mint on gold and silver coinage.

According to the Mint’s January 15 report, the seventeen-year comparison of gold coinage:

From this seventeen‑year span, 1929’s output was the lowest, and 1915’s the highest.

Over the seventeen years, silver output in 1929 was also the lowest, with only 60.937 million ounces of pure silver coined. Thus the contraction in U.S. gold and silver coinage significantly impacts national wealth.

Both Russia and Italy Pay Attention to Sericulture 220

The Soviet government, in order to prepare for the revitalization of the silk industry, recently sent Mr. Khupykhaer, director of the Turkestan State Silk Industry Corporation, to Japan to investigate sericulture. It is reported that in that country the silk industry in southern Russia shows more promise, with annual cocoon outputs of 8,000,000 kg in the Caucasus, 2,000,000 kg in Ukraine, and 15,000,000 kg in Turkestan, totaling 35,000,000 kg. Of this, about 6,000,000 kg are exported to Italy. It is now planned to adopt Japanese-style rearing methods in southern Russia, organized as the Kashmir government in India has done: the government distributes silkworm eggs to farmers, who may purchase them at a very low fixed price, thereby reducing production costs. The aim is to capture the Central European market and become the world’s second‑largest sericulture nation.

From Rome it is reported that, in order to increase domestic wheat yields and expand the silk industry, Italian Prime Minister Mussolini, in view of the Soviet plan mentioned above, is expected to formulate incentive measures rewarding anyone producing as much as 58 kg of cocoons, thereby setting a new national record for sericulture. Cocoon output last year increased by 1,000,000 kg over the previous year. Going forward, it is intended to rear silkworms also in summer and autumn, with the aim of increasing output by 10,000,000 kg. The achievements of recent years are as follows (unit: kilograms):

1929

Cocoons: 53,348,835

Raw silk: 4,830,000

1926

Cocoons: 4,231,000

Raw silk: 3,885,000

Intelligence Testing of Cats and Dogs 221

Students in the Animal Psychology Department of Columbia University have conducted intelligence tests on cats. This year the university will open a spacious new experimental laboratory in Schermerhorn Hall, comprising nine rooms and equipped with an experimental zoo housing dogs, cats, mice, birds, monkeys, and other animals for testing purposes. According to the laboratory’s director, Professor Wharton, intelligence tests on kittens will continue, and it is hoped that the experimental work will be intensified this year.

The results of the feline intelligence tests indicate that wild cats are more intelligent than pet domestic cats. The testing method employs two specially made wire cages, one large and one small, with the small cage placed inside the larger. Milk is placed in the small cage, and to obtain it the cat in the large cage must overcome the obstacle between them. Some cats learn to go around the barrier, while others do not; some are faster, others slower, and their differences in intelligence are judged by their speed.

In addition to this test, more difficult experiments are underway. When comparing the intelligence of various animals, Wharton says that the dogs tested in France by Mr. Xuefu are the most intelligent animals. Dogs can learn many tricks and perform complex sequences of actions, which other species cannot. (Rhyme)
